SAN FEANCISCO, WEDNESDAY. SEPTEMBER 13, 1899.
THE LATE CORNELIUS YANDERBILT.
Cornelius Vanderbilt Jr. and
Harry Payne Whitney and his
: wife arrived at the Vanderbilt
mansion late in the afternoon.
Mrs. Cornelius Vanderbilt Jr. re
mained at Newport. To-night
all of Mr. Vanderbilt's children
were in the city with the excep
tion oi Alfred, and he will un
douhtedly come home.
The home-coming of Cornelius
Vanderbilt Jr. was an unusually
; sad one. The estrangement be
tween him and his father because
of his marriage with Miss Wilson
had never worn away. ()f the re
lations between father and son
Senator Depew says: "They
were on fair terms. I think it is
a week ago to-day that I saw
them together at Newport."
Mr. Depew stated to-night
that the funeral would take place
at 10:30 o'clock Friday morning
from St. Bartholomew's Church.
A special boat will carry the
body, family and friends from the
foot of West Forty-second
street to Staten Island. The
burial will be in Newdorp, where
the Yanderbilt mausoleum is,
and where lie several members of
the family, including Commo
dore Vanderbilt, the founder of
the great fortune.

SAMUEL BRAUNHART IS
CHARGED WITH PERJURY
Accused of Having Sworn Falsely Against Ex-
Senator W. J. Dunn in a Damage Suit.
Bart Burke and John F. McGovern Swear That the Port Warden
Deliberately Misrepresented Them on the Witness-Stand.
Other Prosecutions Will Follow.
SAMUEL BRAUNHART, Port War
den, politician, agitator and , re
former, self-styled, has been ac- i
cused of the serious charge of per- â¢
jury. Braunhart's predicament is !
the outcome of his own enthusiastic i
desire to turn a political trick and to |
throw discredit upon an opponent who i
was once a friend and adviser in Demo- j
cratic politics south of Market street. The j
accuser of Braunhart is ex-Senator Wil- j
Ham J. Dunn, whose reputation, it will be |
remembered, was recently a matter for j
judicial determination before Judge Hunt.
Several months ago the Examiner, with
its customary freedom with facts, pub
lished something which was n->t true in
reference to Dunn. The Examiner de
clared in one of its news items that Dunn
had acted as? referee of a particularly dis
gusting prize-fight.
Dunn is very frank to admit that he will |
attend prize-fights whenever the inclina
tion to do so seizes him. He will even
admit that he bets on prize-fights, but he
Insists? that he has conscientious scruples |
against acting as referee for the best two
pluguglies that over battered one another J
in the ring. And when the matter earne
before Judge Hunt ex-Senator Dunn
swore that he had not even attended the j
prize-fights to which the Examiner made
false reference. Dunn was awarded $500
as a soothing balm to his reputation.
During the trial Samuel Braunhart was
a witness against Dunn. The Port Warden
took the stand as an expert on reputations
In general and that of- Dunn in particular.
It was here that Braunhart laid the foun
dation for his undoing. He swore that the j
reputation of Dunn for truth, honesty and |
integrity was bad. He said he knew so
himself and others had told him that they
knew so. Whr-n pressed to give the names
of his informants he mentioned among
others ex-Senator Bart Burke of Santa
Cruz, and now of this city, and John F.
McGovern. It would have been wiser for
SAMUEL BRAUNHART, ACCUSED OF PERJURY.
Braunhart if he had forgot the names.
Dunn was shocked, or pretended to be.
at the testimony of Braunhart. The two
had long been close political and social
friends. They moved and had their being
upon the same, political plane. They
fought on the same .side together and
what one received the other shared. But
that was long ago when Braunhart was
familiarly and affectionately known as
'â¢Buggy-robe Sammy" in token of a
touching tribute which he had made to
Buckley.
But while politics makes strange bed
fellnws it also makes strange enemies and
Braunhart and Dunn became foes. "Bug
gy-robe Sammy" became a sobriquet of
contempt, and Dunn became one of the
push. This lasted for a while and then
another turn of the political wheel sent
Dunn and Braunhart flying into the same
bed together. Both were in the State
Senate when James H. Budd was elected
Governor. Budd was a reform Governor
and Sammy was a reformer. The sad epi
sode of the buggy-robe had been buried
among the forgotten mistakes of politi
cal history.
Braunhart wanted to be Registrar of
Voters for San Francisco. He considered
that a long anl profitable experience with
touthside ballot boxes qualified him for
the position, but in his own distinguished
self he did not possess the necessary in
fluence to obtain it. He sought, therefore,
and obtained the support of Senators
Dunn. Mitchell, Toner, Blggy and Hen
derson. They pleaded with Budd, showed
how thoroughly and persistently Braun
hart had worked in the cause of reform
and gave every assurance in the world
thai the buggy robe was only an ordinary
affair after all.
Pleading was in vain. Budd had chosen
some one else for the place and Dunn and
Braunharfs other champions were told
that the position of Port Warden would
be given to Braunhart if he would retire
from the fight for Registrar. He refused
to do so" until Hinton was appointed.
Then congratulating himself that he had
defeated Andy Clunie he said he wou'd
accept the position of Port Warden.
Dunn and the others again importuned
Budd and Braunhart got the place.
There was a look of sadness therefore
on the face of Dunn when he heard
Braunhart tell what- a bad man he is.
It was a blow from an old friend. Braun
hart left nothing to be desired In his tes
timony. He was eager, emphatic, preju
diced. He was positive and uncompro
mising-. There was no question about It;
Dunn was bad all over. Braunhart swore
that Dunn's general reputation in the
community for truth, honesty and integ
rity Is bad. He knew so himself, yet he
swore that he was unfriendly with Dunn.
The witness declared that he had heard
Dunn's reputation generally discussed
and all said it was bad.
In particular, and here Braunhart over
?Â«pp:ied the bounds of prudence, he said
that he had heard ex-Senator Bart Burke
and John F. McGovern give Dunn a bad
reputation. Both of these men have taken
an oath before the Superior Court tnat
they did nothing of the sort. They swear
that thc-y never spoke to Braunhart in
reference to Dunn and never mentioned
Dunn'? reputation, good or bad, to Braun
hart. Upon these affidavits, which make
the iffaii an extremely serious one for
Brau:ihart, the complaint of perjury has
been based by Dunn.
Braunhart gave his testimony the lat
ter part of last month and Dunn imme
diately began an investigation. He found
that Braunhart had used the names of
at lea-it two men without authority and
in doing so had not only trifled with the
Superior Court, but had made himself
liable for perjury.
The first man whom Dunn saw was ex-
Senator Bart Burke, who swore to the
following affidavit to prove that he had in
no way discussed Dunn's reputation with
Braunhart:

County of San Francisco, State of Cali

PLEASURE YACHT ON A
ROUND-THE-WORLD CRUISE
Norma of the Atlantic Yacht Club is
Now at Honolulu and Will
Come to This Port.
HONOLULU, Sept. s.â The yacht
Norma. one of the crack vessels of tha
Atlantic Yarht Club of New York, ar
rived in port yesterday after having cov
ered nearly 40,000 miles on a pleasure
cruise under Commodore W. J. Weaver
of the club. â¢ The Norma touched a.t
Niihau and Waimea and will visit the
other Islands of the group before con
tinuing her cruise. She will be over
hauled here and will probably be in port
several weeks, after which she will go to
the South Sea Islands, carrying out the
commodore's purpose to cruise all over
the globe.
Thf Norma left Now York four 3*ears
ago. The little craft touched at Trieste
during the Spanish-American War. and
was held there from May to December
of last year. P'r.'m there she went to
the Red Sea. where she weathered very
severe storms. She Bailed !â¢â  Singapore,
Hongkong and Yokohama, leaving the
latter port for Honolulu on August 5.
NO SECRET TREATIES
WITH FOREIGN POWERS
Secretary Hay Denies That a Secret
Alliance Has Been Entered Into
With England.
COLI'MBI'S. Ohio, Sept. 12.â 1n a let
ter to Chairman Dick of the Republican
State Executive Committee. Hon. John
Hay, Secretary of State, makes this em
phatic statement regarding the alleged
secret alliance between England and the
United States:
"There is no alliance with England nor
with any power und^r the heaven except
thr'se known and published to the world,
the treaties of ordinary international
friendship for the purpose of business
and commerce. No treaty other than
those exists: none has been suggested on
the other side; none is in contemplation.
It has never entered into the mind of the
President nor any of the Government to
forsake, under any inducement, the wis^
precept and example of the fathers, which
forbade entangling alliances with Euro
pean power?."

CORNELIUS
VANDERBILT DIES
VERY SUDDENLY
Continued from First Vnec
watchword and conservatism his guid
ing star, ho worked for four years.
Then he became treasurer of the Har
-1-m Railroad.
It was about this time that Cornelius
Vanderbilt married. The Vanderbilts
for four generations have married
young, Cornelius was 23 years old
when he wedded Miss Alice Gwynne,
daughter of a Cincinnati lawyer. At
that time he was laying the foundation
f<>r a groat business career and for do
jnesti' happiness. He was also walking
In the path of charity and piety. He
was a member of the Protestant TCpis
' church, leader of a Sunday-school
and a member of the Young Men's
Christian Association. He established
a rule that no one was too poor to bo
helped, and in time the demands of
charity and philanthropic service took
almost as many of his hours as did the
rare of the vast property that eventu
ally fell to his direction. There was no
public-spirited movement in which he
did not take part; there was no great
charity in which he was not a partici
pant.
Few knew what he did in this field,
but now and then by accident 'he pub
lic got an insight into his doings in the
field of religion and relief. It was not a
mere coincidence that the day his father
was stricken the messenger summoning
him found Cornelius Vanderbilt attend
ing a meeting of the directors of the
Episcopal mission. Nor was it a co
incidence that he was sitting. with the
trustees of St. Luke's Hospital when
he was informed of the sudden death of
his brother-in-law, Colonel Elliott F.
Shepard.
Cornelius Vanderbilt, dying in 1877,
left a fortune estimated at $90,000,000 v
and by his will placed William H. Van
derbilt at the head of the Vanderbilt
system, which was already becoming
great. Cornelius Vanderbilt was then
43 years old, but he had been In busi
ness continuously for twenty-seven
years. William H. Vanderbilt became
president of the Harlem and New York
Central railroads, and Cornelius first
vice president.
Cornelius soon assumed financial con
trol of both properties. In time Wil
liam H. withdrew from the presidency
and created a board of control of thÂ»
reorganized system, and Cornelius be
(ame chairman of the boards of the
New York Central and Lake Shore and
Michigan Central railroads. This meant
that, subject to his father's direction,
he was ruler of these properties. He
had a salary of $50,000. To this may be
added the house that hau be#n gi\en
him and some stock that gave an In
come of $10,000 a year, but he was inde
pendent of this. Commodore Vanderbilt
had left him $5,000,000, and this he
doubled by speculation in Wall street.
Gradually, as befitting his enhanced
I merit for the exemption of church property
from taxation.

press Their Approval of the

SACRAMENTO, Sept. 12.â The second .
! week of the State Fair promises to excel j
1 the first in all respects. Greater crowds j
j are arriving, and there are accommoda- j
' tions for all. Grangers' day to-day was
1 a great success. The farmers evidently |
came to criticize on all aides, and they
1 declare they are satisfied that the State
1 Fair la a great benefit to the State and
i ought to be availed of by every section.
j They made a heavy demand for books,
I statistics and documents that the law
j requires the State board to gather and
compile.
Never before has such a profound in- j
I terest been manifest by farmers In the i
fair. They take especial Interest in the j

! did herds at the park, in the meetings j

They have, by inquiry to-day, been un

j and find that racing is not its chief inter- j

i mals. The stock grounds were thronged j

In the fairs of the future. The present
favorable condition <>f affairs is clue to the I

tural Society, in conjunction with tho
work of the local Citizens' State Fair'
Club.

STEAMER THERMOPYLAE
IS A TOTAL WRECK
Grounded at the Entrance of Cape Town
Harborâ The Missing Waikato Spoken.
CAPE TOWN, Sept. 12.â The British steamer Thermopylae, Captain i
Throop, bound for Sydney, N. S. W., via other Australian ports, from
London went ashore at the entrance to this port this morning and late
this afternoon broke in two from the force of the waves. The passengers
and crew and a quantity of specie which she had on board were landed
with difficulty. The Th^rmopylea is of 3711 tons. She is a steel vessel
and was built in 1891. She hails from Aberdeen. ,
LITTLETON, N. Z., Sept. 12.â The Spanish bark Aalborg. Captain
Klausen, from Hamburg, May 29, which has arrived at this port, reports
that she spoke the overdue British steamer Waikato, from London.
May 4, for Littleton, August 2, in latitude 39 south, longitude 39 east,
with her shaft broken. Otherwise all was well with the vessel. The
letters of the Waikato were transferred to the Aalborg.

JUDGE CROWLEY SUES
LADY FRANCES BROOME
Brings Action to Collect Money Al
leged to Be Due for Attorney's
Fees.
SANTA BARBARA. Sept. 12.â Judge J.
T. Crowley hasbrought suit against Lady
Frances Broome for $2750 for attorney fees
in the recent trial of the people vs.
Broome, in which the defendant was
found guilty of interfering with and re
sisting an officer In the discharge of his
duties.
The title of the present suit is Wilford
E. Farrel vs. Frances Broome, Crowley
having assigned his claim to Farrel. He
claims $2500 for attorney fees and $250 for
moneys spent by him during the Broome
trial, and which sho requested him to
expend. The papers were served on Mrs.
Broome to-day, and she left this after
noon for San Francisco to secure counsel.

Sequel to an Elopement

Special Dispatch to The Call.
I.ONGVIEW, Texas, Sept. 12.â Panola
County, especiaJly Beckville, is excited
/terrible triple tragedy. The trouble
started with the marriage of James For
eythe. a highly respected young man, and
Miss Ullie Simpson, a be-lle of the neigh
borhood.
It seems that Forsythe did not have the
approval of the glrl'Â» father. Andrew
Simpson, for the hand of his daughter,
but with the assistance of several young
men !n the neighborhood lie succeed-! m
eloping with the young lady and they were
married Saturday nignt. This so angered
the old gentleman that when ho learned
of the wedding last evening: he saddled
up his horse and loaded a double-barreled
shotgun and took his pistol, declaring he
would kill the whole party concerned.
As he role up to Forsythe's home he saw
his son-in-law fleeing through the or
chard He brought him down with seven
buckshot in the groin, leaving him for
dead. He then rode toward the house.
J hhk H Forsythe, father of James, came
out to s^e what the trouble was. and was
tly killed with a load of buckshot in
. bd'omen. Simpson then dismounted
and ran Into the house with his pistol.

.;â  of young Forsythe ran between
them and held hip pistol until the girl
was helped away. Simpson then walked
out to the front gate, put UÂ»Â« Pistol to his
sad and killed himself instantly.
Young Forsythe is desperately wounded.
but may recover. All stores in Beckvlllo
fire closed and business in general is sus-
I on account of the tragedy.

EX-PREMIER FELIX JULES MELINE.
Leader of the Anti-Revision party who is seeking the overthrow
of the present Ministry.
M. Mellne Is fil years of age and has been prominent in French politics
slne*> IBfT6, when he \v;is elected to the Chamber of Deputies, where he be
came a recognized leader. He wa.s Minister of Agriculture In the Jules
Ferry Cabinet (1883-85) and president of the Chamber in ISS9. He Is a bimetal
list and such an ardent protectionist as to earn the sobriquet of the "French
McKlnley." On April 2S, ]S!*>, nx the request of President Faure. he formed
a Cabinet in place of that of M. Bourgeois, which enjoyed a longer existence
than any other Cabinet Blnce the organization of the Third Republic â two
years and one month -the average life of a French Cabinet being but nine
months. During its incumbency the Meline Cabinet is said to have stood
llh interpellations in the Chamber of Deputies. The spring elections of ]R9B
having bee.ii unfavorable to the Government and its candidate for the presi
dency of the Chamber receiving a majority of but one vote. M. Meiine, on
June IS, handed to President Faure the resignation of himself and colleagues,
a new Cabinet being formed on the 2Sth by M, Brisson.
In the lone light for revision of the Dreyfus case M. Meline has been one
of its most violent opponents. On the death of President Faure he was nomi
nated by the "Progressive Republicans" in opposition to M. Emilp Loubet.
The Vatican Influence was rumored to be in his favor, and in spite of the
fact that on tha day of the election he announced his withdrawal in favor of
M. Loubet, ho received 27.< fotes out of Â£12 in the one ballot cast.
M. Meline had commended himself to the "Nationalist" legislators and
a large section of the Monarchists by his antagonism to Dreyfus and the
revision of his ca.se. He is considered one of the ablest men In public life
In France to-day, but his probable triumph bodes no good to Dreyfus.

MISS WELL
FOUND NEAR
HER HOME
! Wandered About for Nearly
Two Days in a Demented
Condition.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
COLFAX, Sept. -12.â Lander, a small
station three miles west of Colfax, was
j thrown Into a great excitement yester
i day by the mysterious disappearance of
i Miss Rhoda B. Howell, daughter of \
John G. Howell, proprietor of the well
known summer resort, Menopolla.
Miss Howell started out for a walk j
Sunday evening about fi:3o o'clock. She '
said she was going for a short walk I
and would return soon. In about an
hour it had grown dark, and the fam
! ily. wondering why she did not return,
j began to call her. Receiving no an
swer, they searched the premises, with
out finding any trace of her. The neigh
bors were notified and the search was
kept up all that night. In the morning
Mr. Howell came to Colfax and report
j cd.the disappearance to the authorities,
â¢ and a number of men were detailed to
hunt for the girl. They kept up the
| search all day Monday and Monday
night, without success. The Yuba ditch
was emptied, on the chance that she
! might have fallen into it and been
i drowned.
This morning the flrebell was rung in
Colfax to gather a crowd, and every
[â person who could possibly go started
ito search the hills. They met with suc
i cers, for shortly after 10 o'clock Miss
| liowell was found not a quarter of a
| mile from her home on the county road
in an almost demented condition. When
j found her hair, which is very long, was
; hanging loose and was matted. Her
I dress was torn to shreds, and her shoes
were almost cut to pieces, showing that
; she had been wandering night and day
since she was lost.
Miss Howell said this evening that
sli" started out for a short walk. She
hart not gone far when it began to
grow dark. She then turned toward
i her home, but lost the path. She says
I she had not gone far until she fell, and
j remembers nothing until she was found
this morning. Miss Howell is resting
easily, and has a chance of speedy re
covery if brain fever does not set in
from the shock. She Is a beautiful girl,
16 years of age, highly accomplished,
and Is the belle of Placer County. Ev
ery one is rejoicing at her recovery.

NOTE LIKE
ULTIMATUM
Transvaal Consents to Con
ference and a Peaceful
Settlement Predicted.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
LONDON, Sept. 13.â A special dis
patch from Pretoria says the British
note which was read in both Raads
j yesterday (Tuesday) Is regarded there
!as a virtual ultimatum. The corre
j spondent says: It proposes a flve
i years' franchise, a quarter representa
tion for the gold fields In the Volks
raad, equality for the British and
Dutch in the Volksraad and equality
for the old and new burghers in re
! gard to presidential and other elections.
If these conditions are accepted a
! conference between representatives of
j the two governments shall follow for
i the purpose of drafting the necessary
I measures and of avoiding the introduc-
I tion of unnecessary conditions by the
â  Transvaal Government or the possibil-
I ity of passage of any new bills calcu
â  la'ted to defeat the reforms.
"In view of the fact,"' the note goes
on to say, "that the present state of
affairs in Sout"h Africa cannot be pro
| longed the definite acceptance of this
j proposal is demanded without delay.
Otherwise her Majesty's Government
will immediately take the whole situa
tion under reconsideration and will act
so as to bring about a settlement."

' settlement.

PEACEFUL SOLUTION
NOW SEEMS PROBABLE
PRETORIA, Sept. 12.â The reading In
the Volksraad to-day of the reply of the
Uritlsh Secretary of State for the Colo
nies. Joseph Chamberlain, to the latest
Transvaal note, was greeted with silence
except for some Interruptions from Mr.
Tosen and other ultra-conservative mem
bers. Its terms, however, have produced
considerable excitement.
There Is no Indication as yet of the
views held regarding it in official circles.
The Yolksraad lias instructed the Gov
ernment to dismiss Mr. de Jonge. Secre
tary of Education, for writing a letter to
the "newspapers accusing the ministers of
tho I'nlted Dutch church of Jingoism be
cause they wanted more English educa
tion In the state schools.
LONDON, Sept. 12. -Owing to the un
derstanding that Mr. Chamberlain's note
is to be published simultaneously in
London and Pretoria, only an inadequate
summary has yet been telegraphed. The
Iyindnn papers are Inclined to withhold
comment until the text is available.
Despite the fact that the note is de
scribed as an ultimatum, the balance of
opinion tends to regard it as a rather
moderate document, since, apparently, it
does not fix a definite time limit, leaves
the real crux of the dispute for the pro
posed conference and does not raise the
question of suzerainty. In a word, the
note seems to leave the way open to
further negotiations.
The Times, which considers the sum
mary obviously important, says: "It Is
Incredible that all mention of suzerainty
and the other matters In controversy
have been passed over and ignored."
A special dispatch from Pietermaritz
burg, capital of Natal, says: "President
Kruger, It Is asserted in reliable quarters.
Is prepared to attend the proposed con
ference at Cape Town unconditionally,
provided he Is assured that Mr. Chamber
lain's latest dispatch represents all that
will be demanded by Grent Britain. A
peaceful solution, therefore, is highly
probable." ____^___________

Situation in Florida Remains the

Abates Somewhat.
JACKSONVILLE, Fla., Sept. 12.â The
reports (if two physicians at Key West
to the State Hoard of Health to-day show

fever and one death at that plaoe. The
situation at Miami remains the same and
the panic threatened at Port Tampa has
subsided. Several persons who were ex
posed have been carefully isolated, hut
the period of Incubation has expired and
there Is no fear of them developing a
case of yellow fever.

BRITISH AUTHORITIES
DETAIN THE TARTAR
Transport Held at Hongkong on the
Ground of Being Overcrowded.
Special Cable to the Call and New York Herald. Copyrighted, 1599, by James Gordon Bennett.
HONGKONG, Sept. 12.â The British authorities here have d_etained the
United States transport Tartar on the ground that she is overcrowded. She
will not be permitted to sail unless she leaves 450 men behind under the
present ruling. American Consul General Wildman maintains that the Tar
tar, being an American troop ship, is not subject to British laws. The
British authorities are now considering the protest which Mr. Wildman has
filed.
Formal complaint has been lodged with the American Consul General by
discharged soldiers on board against the condition of the Tartar.
WASHINGTON, Sept. 12.â Secretary Root said this evening that he had
received no advices regarding the action of Hongkong authorities in de
taining the transport Tartar because of her alleged overcrowded condition.
Brigadier General Ludington. Quartermaster General, said this after
noon that the Tartar was loaded at Manila hy General Otis. She had 1230
men on board, and this number is not sufficient to overcrowd her. Even if
she were overcrowded, he said the British authorities could have no ground
for demanding that 450 men be removed from the ship, as she Is an Amer
ican naval vessel and subject to American jurisdiction only. If the Gov
ernor or Hongkong has detained the Tartar, representations will probably
be made to Great Britain on the subject.
The tribune of arbitration at Geneva held that "the privilege of extra
terrltorialty, accorded to vessels of war, had been admitted into the law of
nations," and under this provision the Tartar, which is a Government
transport, is amenable only to American laws.
No trouble is anticipated by the authorities in securing the release of the
Tartar with all men placed on board the ship at Manila, but when she ar
rives at San Francisco full reports will be expected from the officers on
board respecting the trouble they have had, and especially the reason for
proceeding to Hongkong instead of to Nagasaki.
CAPTAIN DYER
GIVEN A SWORD
Honor for Commander
of Baltimore.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
BALTIMORE. Sept. 12.-Captain N.
Mayo Dyer, commander of the cruiser
Baltimore in the memorable conflict of
May 1, IS9B, at Manila, was honored by
this city to-day. Residences and business
houses were gay with bunting, the streets
were jammed with people wearing"Dyer"
buttons and "Dyer" badges, and all the
ships in the harbor, regardless of nation
ality, were bedecked In his honor.
Soon after breakfast Captain Dyer was
taken in charge by a special committee
and entertained >in various ways until
1:30 p. m.. when he was escorted to the
City Hall to be welcomed by a reception
committee consisting of about 200 of
Baltimore's most prominent people, head
ed by Mayor Malster. Meantime a pro
cession, composed of the Fourth and Fifth
regiments of the Maryland National
Guard, the Naval Reserve Corps, the
Fifth Regiment (veteran corps), two com
panies of United States artillery regulars
from Fort McHenry, the Maryland divi
sion of. the Grand Army of the Republic,
and many uniformed civic and semi
military organizations, paraded through
the principal streets of the city, passing
the City Hall, where it was reviewed by
Captain Dyer. Upon an Immense platform
3000 school children were so arranged as to
form an Immense imitation of the Ameri
can flag. When the parade had .passed
Mayor Malster, on behalf of the City
Council of Baltimore and of the citizens
generally, presented to Captain Dyer a
set of resolutions commending his conduct
at Manila and his record as an officer.

Sports
IN ONE ROUND
McGOVERN WINS
FROM PALMER
Brooklyn Lad Defeats the Eng
lishman, Gaining a World's
Championship Thereby.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
NEW YORK, Sept. 12.â Terry McGov
crn .of Brooklyn whipped â¢Pedlar Palmer
of London thoroughly in less than one |
round in the arena of the Westchester
Club near Tuckahoe, X. T., this after
noon. With the victory went the title of
bantam champion of the world, although J
both contestants were actually In the
feather-weight class.
The Englishman, although forcing the
pace at the outset, was really never in it,
and McGovern simply battered his .oppo
nent Into partial insensibility. The Brook- i
lyn lad's arms worked llko piston-rods I
and the punishment which he meted out |
to Palmer was swift and terrible.
The battle did not last quite one round,
the actual time of fighting being exactly i
2 minutes 22 seconds, at the end of which
time Palmer was lying helpless though
half conscious on the floor of the ring.
Through an unlooked-for accident on the .
official timer's part, the lads were sep
arated by the gong when they had been
lighting one minute. Bach went to his
corner, but the error was rectified in less
than twelve seconds and the boys resumed
hostilities in the center of the ring im
mediately at the command of the referee,
George Siler. The ringing of the gong
was as purely accidental as it was un
looked for, and the Englishman's handlers
made no complaint on this score.
From the outset McGovern seemed to
have his man well In hand, but his most
sanguine admirers never Imagined that
Terry would win in such short order. It
was a case of a hurricane fighter pitted
against a clever boxer, and the fighter
won. McGovern Is a veritable fighting
machine and he went at his adversary j
with bulldog determination and lightning |
rapidity. His arms were never at rest
for a moment and he beat down Palmer's
guard with his sledgehammer blows. The
ring where the battle took place was
pitched in the center of a stockade, in
which seats were arranged for the ac
commodation of 10,000. The structure is
built on the same principle as that in
which Fitzsimmons and Corbett fought at
Carson City. The prices of admission ;
ranged from $3 to ?!.'. and seven-eighths
of the seating capacity of the inclosure
was occupied.
The purse for which the lads contested
was $10,000. 75 per cent of which went to
the winner and the remainder to the
loser. In addition to this both pugilists
are guaranteed an interest in the privf
leges ruing from the kinetoecope pic
tures of the tight.
A more representative crowd of snort
ing men than that which gathered at
Tucka this afternoon never watched
a pugilistic contest. Of course the pugil
istic element was largely represented,
every boxer of note, from the top-notch
ers in the heavy- weight division to the
paper-weights in the amateur ranks, be.
ing ">n hand. Among the big fellows at
the ringside were Tom Sharkey, Bob Kitz
simmons, Kid Mcoy and Jim Corbett.
Each of this Quartet met with a warm
reception, but when John L. Sullivan
walked down the aisle and took a seat
in a box at the ringside a mighty cheer
greeted him, and the big fellow bowed
his ackuowledgments with a hearty greet
ing. Sullivan went to McGuvern"s dress
ing-room and gave the little fellow some
sound advice, which undoubtedly bene
fited McGovern, as the subsequent events
proved. ']"..â  were hundreds of port*
ing men from all over the United States,
and a large number of the racetrack con
tingent was in evidence.
When the boys entered the ring a fair
estimate of the attendance would place
the number at 9000. McGovern was a
strong favorite in the betting, several
large wagers being made at odds of 100
to 80, and later on these odds were in
creased to 100 to 70, at which price all
of the British money in sight was picked
up. McGovern and his manager had sev
eral commissioners betting for them.
and they won about $8000 In the aggre
gate. McGovei weighed 116% and Palm
er 115% when they weighed in at 5 o'clock
yesterday morning. It was understood
that they would have to weigh in at 5
o'clock this morning again, but at the re
quest of Palmer's manager it was mu
tually agreed to have the lads meet at
catch weights without further weighing.

OLYMPIC CLUB
WILL HAVE A
NEW MANAGER
Superintendent W. J. Kennedy to
Be Succeeded by H. C.
Houghton.
William J. Kennedy has resigned his
position as superintendent of the Olym
pic Athletic Club, after having served
that institution during the past seven
years. H-- will be succeeded on October 1
by H. C. Houghton. an insurance man.
who is prominently Identified with the
Bohemian Club. The superintendent is
practically the working head of the club
and possibly, unconsciously, has much to
do in framing its policy.
The Olympic Club was founded on ama
teur athletics and all the fame it ever
gained was through its amateur runners,
gymnasts, boxers and cyclers. In recent
it has in a degree turned from these
'and has lost prestige. The men now at
the head of affairs are going to com
mence anew and will devote all their
energies to the upbuilding of amateur
â¢ . getting as far away from the Pro
fessional element and its following as
possible. It will be essentially a gentle
men's club and will be maintained on a
T*o this end the dues, now $3 per month.
will be increased to $5 on the Ist of next
month. There may be a slight loss of
membership owing to this radical move,
but the directorate believes the Ing-eased
income wiil more than compensate for the
possible defection of some members. Thy
comforts and conveniences for the memb
ers will be added to at once. One of the
lirst improvements will be a handbau
court of regulation size. This win be
built on the lot adjoining the clubhouse
on the west. It will be well lighted and
airy both of which qualifications are
lacking in the present courts. The Bpace
now occupied by these will be converted
into a lounging room for members. It is
Intend! d to keep the lobby clear of loung
it is not known yet what changes will
be made among the employes throughout
og. The wishes pf the new
rintendent will be consulted m this,
and only such men as are congenial to
bim Will be retained.

fortable margin, with Mesmerist at the

rounded the upper turn, however, he j
gradually cut down Modrine' S lead and
when straightened out for .home was in
front.

Merchants as Anglers.
The members of the Merchants' Rock
cod Club made one of their regular trips

Brisk. The members in the party were:

SAN TOMAS
IS WINNER OF
THE FUTURITY
Crack Two-Year-Old of the Burns
& Waterhouse Stable Comes
In at Sacramento Track.
SACRAMENTO. Sept. 12.â 1t was Gran
gers' day at Agricultural Park this after
noon and the visitors from the rural dis
tricts were among the early comers to the
track. Long before the gates were
opened the price of gold bricks had taken
an upward tendency, but the market was
dull with little doing. The patrons of
husbandry were attentive lookers-on, but
speculated little. The weather was warm
as usual and mosqirito-net clothing is still
in much demand.
Speculating on the harness events was
uncertain as usual. Dolly D won the 2:19
trot and it was hard telling whether or
not 3he was favorite. Dlawood at remu
nerative odds won the 2:12 class pace.
In the running events the talent fared
better, three choices scoring.
The rich Futurity stake for two-year
olds of the running division was the stel
lar event of the programme. Just a year
ago the stable of Burns & Waterhouse
carried off the prize with Bonibel, and
to-day repeated the turn with San Tomas,
a son of Imp. Brutus and Deception. The
winner, coupled in the betting with Bee
Bee, ruled odds on choice, the only other
starters being The Echo and Constella
tor. Bee Bee, with Charley Thorpe up.
could have won hands down, but was
! eased up at the finish to permit her stable
: companion to earn the laurels. The Echo
seemed possessed of no speed whatever
I and Constellator looked outclassed.
The 2:19 class trot, first decided, was
i captured by Dolly D. the Santa Rosa
j stock farm representative. In the open
ing heat the mare, a 1 second choice in the
betting, ended up in third position, owing
to a disastrous break early in the game.
Thereafter the daughter of Sidney Dillon
seemed to have her field at her mercy,
for she won hands clown. Athavls, han
dled by Dan Mizner, took the first heat
at odds of 8 to 1, thus cinching second
money. Twilight, guided by Dick Havey,
won third money.
Hoppled, distrusted and disgraced in
his last time out, Dlawood, a son of Di
ablo, downed his field like breaking sticks
in the 2:12 class pace. The books, not
[ noted for generosity, laid 8 to 1 against
the pacer for the opening heat, which he
i won easily from the favorite, Don. Beach
wood was also out for a "killing," but
after leading to the paddock stopped to
a walk. The latter was really the "good
thing" of the afternoon. In the second
heat Arthur W came from the bunch
with a fine turn of speed, but Diawood
developed no backing up propensities, |
taking the second heat and race from Ar
thur quite as easily as the first In 2:11. ,
I 1 this heat I Direct made a game effort,
finishing third, and with more racing luck ,
would have been a hardy contender at â 
' the end. It was perhaps fortunate for the j
winner that the conditions of the race |
did not call for heats three In five.
After the Futurity had been decided, a
seven furlong sprint, for which Manzanilla j
closed favorite, was called. Thorpe, who !
had the mount, rode one of his old-time â 
finishes, getting the verdict at the wire i
I by a short head from Meadow Lark, with
Mor?e up.
The three-cornered owners handicap,
with Libertine, Hohenzollern and Mount
McGregor as starters, was no doubt the
fiercest betting feature of the day. Mc-
Gregor, with Thorpe up, had the call, and
his superb saddle work undoubtedly won.
Assuming the '.cad almost from the jump,
he was hard pressed at the wire, but the
bay with roan markings hung on gamely,
heating Hohenzollern out over two
i lengths in 1:47*4. Had Morse on . the
latter, been given a whip the result
might have been different. Jack Ward,
astride Libertine, searched for all the
I pockets and found them. He . should re

This will be the fourth contest for the
perpetual challenge cup, which was pur
chased by subscription in 1595 and de
posited with the Encinal Yacht Club as
trustee. In that year the San Francisco
Yacht Club challenged with F. C. Bart
lett's sloop Queen, which was beaten by
the Encinal defender, El Sueno. In 1896
the San Franciscos challenged ag-ain with
the sloop Catherine, but she was defeated
by the Knolnal yacht Fawn. In 1897 the
Corinthians challenged with the Aeolus,
which defeated the Fawn and won the
trophy, which has not been challenged for
until a few weeks ago.
Ex-Commodore J. W. Pew, who will
steer the Truant, is thoroughly familiar
with the Corinthian course, over which
the race will be sailed. He is an excellent
seaman and was commodore of the Corin
thian Yacht Club In 1593. 1895 and 1896.

SILENT TOAST
IN MEMORY OF
HARRY EMERIC
San Francisco Flycasting Club
Listen to a Eulogy by Fish
Commissioner Vogelsang.
The members of San Francisco Flyeast
ing Club drank a silent toast to the mem
ory of Harry F. Emeric at their meeting
last night. Prior to this touching cere
mony the scene was an Impressive one
as the members listened intently to a
gracefully worded eulogy pronounced by
Fish Commissioner Vogelsang on the man
â¢who is 6aid to have done more in his
lifetime for fish and game interests in
California than any other individual. The
toast was drunk standing.
Archie J. Treat presented a series of
views of the trouting streams of the Lak.j
Tahoe and Glen Alpine district. They
seemed in effect to take the anglers iroiti
the wind swept streets of the city into
the mountains they all know so well.
With the aid of a stereopticon. operate â <!
by H. C. Tlbbitts. he showed those in at
tendance favorite pools and lakes in
which they have ofttime cast a fly Â»n
their quest for the splendid trout wnlcn
is there in abundance. t
The majority of the views were from
photographs by Mr. Treat and showed the
most artistic bits of mountain scenery.
Mr Treat adds the accomplishment of an
gler to that of photographer and handled
his subject from the standpoint of the
former. He gave a clever running de
scription of the views, taking pains to
compliment the mc-mbers of the State
Fish Commission for the good work they
have done in storking the streams. TJjose
in attendance were:

ALMOST RUN DOWN
THE YACHT SHAMROCK
Sir Thomas Lipton's Challenger
Nearly Fouled by a Scow That
Drifted in the Wind.
NEW YORK, Sept. 12.â Great peril be
set the Shamrock yesterday afternoon and
evening at the height of the easterly gale.
The scow Ulster, with the spare spars of
the challenger, dragged her anchor, and
was only prevented from drifting down
upon the yacht by the heroic efforts of
the combined crews of the Llpton fleet.
Three attempts were made by the men of
one of the launches before success
crowned their efforts. With heavy lines
fast the barge was slowly towed from her
dangerous proximity to the yacht. Then
lines were thrown to the Shamrock, and
with one around her steel mast near the
deck and another to her mooring buoy she
was dragged to a position of safety.
The Shamrock's topmast, which had
been housed, was sent up. Then the new
main sail was bent and when it was in
place it was readily seen what Sir Thomas
Lipton meant when he said he had sur
prises in store for American yachtsmen.
It is crosscut fashioned, after the biL?
sails turned out by the Herreshoffs. There
were a few rough places about it in need
of an hov.r or two of stretching.
The new boom was variously estimate'!
to be from 110 to 112 feet long, and the
metal gaff at from 69 to 71 feet. All in
all, the new sail is formidable, and. in the
words of naval experts, "lays all over
the Columbia's biggest in size."
Later in the afternoon the new club top
sail was sent up. Its size is also pro
digious. In fact, it is in reality a sky
scraping topsail and Its area is such as to
make all ordinary topsails look modest.

ice" at Belvedere.

10th*BROA DWAYCtcI. main Â»o.}o*kl*noNEWS OF CAMPS AND TRANSPORTS
THE CAMPS ARE
MUCH HEALTHIER
THAN THE CITY
Over Eleven Thou
sand Quartered.
The records of the Presidio for yes
terday morning show that 11,553 men
are quartered within its boundaries â
enough to make an army. There were 330
recruits, 40 casuals, 57 in the regular gar
rison, approximately 5256 volunteer infan
try on the way to the Philippines and ap
proximately 5000 more returned from the
islands ar.d awaiting muster out.
In all this number there is but one case
of typhoid fever, and there is no case of
contagious disease that has been contract
ed in the camps. Every case now in the
hospital has en brought in with the
troops. The percentage of sick in the
camps shows a higher health average
than the city, while the death rate is far
below anything the city can bring for
ward. The sanitary arrangements of the
camps have not been equaled in the mili
tary history of the country, but it has
cost a great deal of money. As they now
stand the camps at the Presidio have cost
the Government $150,000, but there has been
no loss of life teat could have been pre
vented, and the investment was a g<x>d
one. Returning volunteers speaJk of the
camp as "home," when compared to those
in Manila, and they are pretty near being
correct.
Governor Lind of Minnesota spent the
afternoon at the Presidio. The $10 due
each man from the State was paid, and
tnen the regiment was formed In line and
was inspected by the Governor, and after
ward the men marched in review before
him.
To-morrow General Shaffer and his
staff will proceed to Sacramento to be
present -: "Governor's day" at the
State Fair. It is expected that Admiral
Kautz and his staff will also be of the
party. It will be a strictly full dress
affair, and the two dignitaries with the
accompanying officers will be given a
special Pullman.
To-day is the time set for the enter
tainment of the Minnesota. Regiment by
the men from California. While out in
Manila the Minnesota Regiment had the
California men over to a Thanksgiving
dinner, and the home boys have never
forgotten the time they had. The affair
to-cay will be In return for the favors
they then received, and efforts will be
made to have it as successful an event
as was the other occasion.
Orders assigning the remainder of the
Thirty-fourth Infantry left after the
Columbia sailed to the Belgian King have
been issued. Thirty unassigned recruits
will also sail on the same transport.
The following officers have been as
signed to duty with the battalion of the
fortieth Infantry to be organized here:
First Lieutenant Henry P. Fletcher.
First tenant Hamilton Bowie and
Second Lieutenant Joseph C. Righter Jr.
First Lieutenant Russell C. Langdon,
Third Infantry, has been assignee to
duty with the recruits at the Presidio
pending assignment to Manila.

JEWISH VOLUNTEERS.
Patriotic Hebrews W ho Served
Their Country Against
Spaniardand TagaJ.
The Twenty-seventh Regiment, United
States Infantry Volunteers. Colonel Bell,
which arrived in San Francisco from New
York last Wednesday, contains quite a
percentage of Jewish young men. A dele
gation of the volunteers called on Rabbi
M. S Levy recently. The Israelites of
the regiment will attend Day o4Â» Atone
ment services at the Geary street Taber
nacle next Wednesday evening. The next
evening., Thursday, they will be enter
tained at a downtown Jewish restaurant.
The next day. Friday, the regimen! sails
per Manila. The alumnae of Beth Israel
fad a number of young ladies will assist
ft entertaining the volunteers at the ban
quet. Rabbi Levy called on Colonel Bell,
commanding the regiment, and received a
cordial welcome and the assurance that
everything that the colonel could do to
promote the welfare of the Jewish volun
teers would be done.

SOUTH DAKOTA'S LEADING
SON ARRIVES IN TOWN
GOVERNOR ANDREW E. LEE of South Dakota arrived at the Occidental
from the north yesterday morning- He oame alone, having been preceded
- c time and another by ail the other gentlemen who compose the del
egation nominated by that State to come out to the coast and welcome the
volunteers.
All the officers of the South Dakota Regiment save those under arrest
congregated at the depot to welcome their executive and accompanied him to
his hotel, where the excellent breakfast which had been prepared for him was
discussed with a gusto furnished by long travel on the part of the Governor
and long deprivation on the part of the officers who sat down to the table with
him.
After breakfast the Governor retired to his apartment?, where he had a long
conference with Colonel Frost, supposedly concerning the troubles that exist
between the commanding officer of the Daicota boys and some of his subor
dinates.
During the aY t fovernor L^e visited the Presidio, where he was wel
comed with all due honor by the men of his State's regiment.
As to the plans concerning the b ~ â¢-- \r.g of th< I . -lunteers they re
main still in abeyance as far as anything definite is concerned. From those of
the delegation who arrived here in time t ~ ; .:sh the object of their jour
ney and greet the boys on their aj-rival it was '.-earned that the wishes of the
men themselves would be the all-importar.t factor in the celebration which is to
take place in their own State. As yet these wishes have not been fully ascer
tained.

Fractured His Skull.
John S. Stawpert. a conductor recently
1 employed by the Market Street Railway
| Company, was struck by a cable car last
i night, while crossing at the corner of Clay
! and Leavenworth streets. He was remov
ed to the Receiving Hospital, where it
was found that he had sustained a frac
ture of the skull. As he was an employe
of the railroad company Dr. Dray, the
surgeon in charg#. had him removed to
the Railroad Hospital. His skull was tre
panned and a large clot of blood removed
from the brain. Although his condition is
serious, the doctors hope for his recovery.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL. WEDNESDAY. SEPTEMBER 13. 1899.
KNIGHTS OF ST.
PATRICK IN MEMORIAM
At a meeting of the Knights of St.
Patrick last night the following resolu
- â â - ere ad< pted:
Whereas. It has pleased Almighty
to take from our circle our dear
respected brother knight. ex-Sen
ator M. J. Donovan, to receive his heav
enly reward; and.
Whereas, He has filled the positions
" 3< hool Director and Sub-Treasurer
for the city and county of San Fran
cisco, as well as Senator for the State of
California, with credit to himself and
his fellow citizens and countrymen;
and.
Whereas, In his death our society has
lost one of its oldest and highly re
spected members, one ever ready to dis
charge the duties pertaining to mem
bership.
Resolved, That, in mourning his loss,
we shall ever revere the memory of a
friend, an honest and an upright man.
who won the respect of all with whom
he came in contact in public or private
life, and who, as a brother knight, was
true to the land that gave him birth â
Ireland.
Resolved, That these resolutions be
spread on the minutes of our meeting
and that a copy be transcribed and for
warded to his widow and family.
JAMES K. COSGRAVE.
THOS. J. WELSH.

CHICAGO ATTORNEY
DIES IN LOS ANGELES
J. T. Kretzinger Succumbs to Heart
Failure in the Hollenbeck
Hotel.
LOS ANGELES. Sept. 12.-J. \ . Kret
zincr. a well-iaiuwu lawyer of Chicago,
was found dead in his bed in a room at
the Hi llenbet k Hotel here to-day. Mr.
Kretzinger arrived yesterday from Den
ver and went to Santa Monica in com
pany with Captain W. T. Beatty. They
returned last night and retired in sep
arate apartments at the Hollenbeck. This
morning Captain Beatty arose late and
not meeting Mr. Kretzinger according to
appointment went to his room. There
weri- no responses to his repeated knocks
and when the door was opened Mr. Kret
zinger was found to be dead. A physician
who was summoned gives the cause of
death as heart disease.
J. T. Kretzinger is a member of the law
firm of Kretzinger, Gallagher & Rooney.
with offices In the Monadnock block, Chi
cago. He came here from Denver on a
mining deal in which Captain Beatty la
interested. When he retired last nigm.
he appeared to be in good health and
spirits.
HOSPITAL CLOSED.
Smallpox Has Been Entirely Eradi
cated in This City.
The smallpox hospital at San Bruno av
enue and Twenty-sixth street was offi
cially closed by the Board of Health yes
terday. The last of the victims of the late
epidemic of thr disease was discharged
from the institution early in the day, and
at 12 o'clock several health inspectors
with apparatus and chemicals fumigated
the building throughout.
Dr. Fryor. the interne of the City and
County Hospital who volunteered to at
tend the smallpox patients at the insti
tution, and who was afterward granted a
fee of $"> a day during his quarantine by
the Board of Health, was discharged this
morning, as were the nurses and other at
tendants.

HIGHBINDER BULLETS
FLY IN CHINATOWN
AFTERMATH OF THE ABDUCTION
CASE.
Wong Hing, the San Jose Murderer.
Fires Three Shots at His
Pursuers.
Wong Hing. a San Jose murderer and
highbinder, took three shots at a couple
of coolie brethren yesterday afternoon
and narrowly escaped killing one of them,
>; at the same time hitting an inoffensive
workingman. who was seated in a store
in the neighborhood. The shooting took
* place on Spofford alley, between Clay and
" Washington streets, and grew out of the
> recent abduction of a Chinese woman
> from Waverly place.
About six weeks ago Wong Hing shot
' and killed Lee On? Poon in San Jose and
Â» also wounded Deputy Sheriff Mackey. who
attempted to arrest him. The trouble at
that time arose from a dispute over a
' woman who had been abducted from this
âº, city. Since the time of the murder, the
whereabouts of Hing has be^n unknown
' to the officers, and yesterday Deputy
s Sheriff Mackey brought two Chinamen.
, Ah Fong and Ah Yum. to this city that
they might make a search of Chinatown
> and possibly locate the man who was
> wanted.
> About 3 o'clock yesterday afternoon the
Chinamen were passing down Spofford
* alley, when the object of their search sud
> denly emerged from a doorway and
, drawing a 44-caliber revolver, fired
three shots at them. The first bullet
> passed through the leg of Ah Yura'a
, trousers and the second, glancing from
the pavement, passed through a window
> of Mow Tung & Co.'s store, at 536 Wash
, . ington street. The last missile grazed the
head of Bin Tun. a laborer who was seat
* ed inside of the window.
> Wong Hing hastily replaced the revolv
er in his pocket and made his escape aft^r
the third shot had been fired. He entered
' ' a building on the alley tn which the shoot
> ing occurred and which Is known to con
tain headquarters of a number of tongs,
and was not seen afterward. The polioe
> made a search of the premises and came
Â» ' to the conclusion that the man had passed
over a number of roofs in the neighbor
' hood and alighted on Waverly plar
> i Chinese are much excited over the affair
, ; aDd claim that it is a good prelude for
another highbinder war.
"OTHELLO" A HAT
AT THE TIVOLI
THE further the grand opera season
at the Tivoli progresses the more in
terested seem the public in the per
formances. "Othello" attracted a
large audience last evening, which ex
pressed its appreciation in frequent out
bursts of honest enthusiasm. Particular
applause was bestowed upon the duet be
tween Othello and lago in the second act.
which Avedano and Salassa rendered with
a dramatic Intensity that brought the
house upon its feet. A regular ovation
occurred after each act.
Miss Anna Lichter ad.ied an^th^r laurel
leaf to her wreath by her exemplary" ren
dition of Desdamona. The "Are Mana"
- xecuted with an adherence :<> senti
ment, a faithfulness to color and phras
ing that added a great many admirers
to Miss LJ BX of friends.
Miss Charlotte Beckwith, although not
playing exactly a prominent part, should
be encouraged. She has a natural dra
matic soprano with some mezzo qua
and if she would study more the art of
bringing her voice forward instead of
keeping it back there cannot be any doubt
that her success as an operatic singer
would be a great one.
Orchestra and chorus added luster to
the performance ar.d the bass solo in the
last H'-t was renedered very intelligently
and effectively.
ALFRED METZGER.
DREYFUS AND BOARD OF TRADE
A Chance That California's Exhibit
â¢ Will Be Sent to Buffalo.
The subject of the Paris International
Exposition and the Dreyfus agitation
formed one of the principal matter? oi
-sion at the meeting of the State
Board of Trade yesterday. It will be re
membered that soon after the State Paris
Commission was formed the genti^rci'-n
composing the latter requested the Board
of Trade to assist in the matter of mak
ing a collection of the products of Cali
fornia that would excel anything that had
ever been placed before the public. Man
ager J. A. Filcher and his corps of assist
ants, at the instance of the directors, s^t
to work, and now the exhibit is well un
der way, but much still remains to be
done. So far the exhibits consist of fruit,
cereals and agricultural products.
Within the last few .lays the protests
of all civilized nations have gone up
against the verdict pronounced against
Captain Dreyfus of th<= French army, and
not a few of the influential bodies of
Europe and America have suggested that
the best way to express their contempt
power where such injustice
is to boycott the Paris exposition.
At the meeting of the board yesterday it
wws decided to wait until the State Leg
islature should take action. As a fact
the State Board of Tradr- stands as an
employe of the State Par-.s i 'ommission.
and has no say in what shall b*> done. Th>
collecting of the exhibits will go on until
the commission mentioned sriv-s the ord-'r
to stop. Should this order be given it is
likely that the fine exhibit already col
iP^r.^V', will be senr to thf> big national ex
position to be held in Buffalo. N. Y.

COLONEL O. F. LONG
HONORED BY COMRADES
ARMY AND NAVY UNION GAR
RISON NAMED AFTER HIM.
Ths Occasion Is Celebrated by a Re
cÂ«ption to the Popular Quarter
master of the Transport
Service.
Colonel Oscar F. Long of the United
States transport service was tendered a
splendid reception Ljy the garrison of the
Army and Navy which bears his name.
The garrison was formerly known as Cal
ifornia No. 101. but in honor of the many
courtesies extended to its members, and
by reason of his exceeding popularity as
a gentleman and a soldier, the name was
changed to the Colonel O. F. Long Garri
son.
The reception took place in the banquet
hall in the Alcazar building, and over 200
of his comrades gathered to do him
honor. These Included the members of
the garrison, delegations from the lowa
and iCarblebead and volunteers from the
various regiments now at the Presidio.
Commander J. F. Fitzgerald introduced
the guest of the evening In an appropriate
speech, and Colonel Long responded by
expressing his sincere appreciation of the
honor conferred on him. He also referred
to the co-operation of the Army and Navy
Union with the transport service, which
had dene much to bring it to its present
high state of efficiency.
speeches were also delivered by Captain
D. T. Geary of the heavy artillery. Past
Commander Frank Wisker. Honorary
Paymaster M. Remensperger, Captains n.
P. Batchelder and John Barnason of the
transport service, Quartermaster J. A.
Lansanderfer, Vice Commodore R. L.
peverell and Garrrison Inspector William
Gross.
The evening- was enjoyably spent in re
counting Incidents of the late and pres
ent wars. There were selections by the j
Third Artillery band and vocal selections ;
by several of the guests, and the enter
tainment concluded with three hearty
cheers for Colonel Long.
TROUBLE ON BOARD
TROOPSHIP PUEBLA
Negro Soldiers Accused of Cheating
at Craps Attacked by the
White Men.
HONOLULU. Sept. s.â Eighty-seven ne
gro soldiers on the transport City of
Puebla. which -rived from San Francisco
this morning, had a few moments of great
excitement and terror last Thursday in
mid-ocean. A quarrel arose between the
whites and the blacks, and only the timely
intervention of a provost guard prevented
a fight that would have ended badly for
th- colored troops.
The trouble arose over "shooting craps."
The negroes were accused of cheating
and stealing and soon a good deal of feel
ing was shown. Last Thursday morning
matters reached a climax. Several white
rs claimed to have been robbed
during the night and accused several of
the coiortd men of the robbery. The ne
groes stood by one another ar.d collected
together in the bow of the boat, ready
for an attack. Before the white men had
time to make an advance Captain Devere
learned of the situation. He sent a pro
vost guard to the scene and the guard
held the factions at bay. For the rest of
the trip the negroes kept together in the
bow and there was no more trouble.
COUGHRAN WILL BE
CHARGED WITH PERJURY
Mrs. Charles Smyth Will Swear to a
Warrant for His Arrest.
SAX RAFAEL. Sept. 12.â At the insti
â  M .ehran. a local bill collec
tor. Arthur Scott swore to a complaint to
day charging Attorney Charles Smyth of
San Francisco with obtaining money un
der false prtenses. It was alleged that
Smyth had drawn a worthless check on
the Columbian Bank for $15. Scott be
came convinced to-night that Coughran
had made of him a tool with which to
vent spite on an enemy, and had the case
dismissed after Mrs. Smyth had paid the
amount of the check before the warrant
could be served.
"In the guise of a friend this man
Coughran has been secretly hounding my
husband for months," said Mrs. Smyth
this evening. "He Is the correspondent for
an evening paper and has used its col
umns to grind his ax in a shameful man
ner. Coughran swore a short time ag >
he had served papers on me that were
never served, and after consulting with
my husband to-morrow. I intend to have
him arrested on a charge of perjury."

THE ANTI-TRUST CONVENTION.
GOVERNOR SAVERS of Texas, who issued
the invitation to the Governors and Attorneys j
General of the various States and Territories
to attend a convention in St. Louis for the purpose
of devising uniform legislation for the suppression of
trusts, is reported to be well pleased with the sup
port given to the movement. If such be the case the
Governor is easily pleased, for while the invitation
was sent out months ago and the time for holding
the 'convention is drawing near, the Governors of but
eleven States besides his own, and but seven Attorneys
General,' have promised to attend. It is true that ten
other Governors have expressed a willingness to be
present it their duties permit, but such expressions
are hardly more than polite methods of declining the
invitation. The convention therefore will fall a long
way short of representing all the States of the Union.
It is notable, moreover, that the Governors who
have promised to attend are nearly all of them
Democrats. The only Republicans of note among j
them are Pingree of Michigan and Tanner of Illinois, j
and neither of these is a representative of genuine
Republicanism. Pingree is something of a political
freak, having almost as many vagaries as a Popu- j
list and being a confirmed believer in many of the
doctrines of state socialism. Tanner is not much (
wiser, and certainly does not represent the conser
vative common sense of his State or of his party.
When the convention assembles it will find itself j
handicapped at the start, for the Governors and At- j
torneys General of twelve States and one or two
Territories cannot take action or adopt any policy <
that will be binding upon all the States. The meet- !
ing will hardly do more than give utterance to the
vague discontents existing among a considerable j
portion of the people concerning trusts. It may pos- :
sibly adopt a set of very strongly worded resolutions,
hut strong words will not stop the organization of
trusts nor even lead to any comprehensive legislation |
against them.
The futility of the proposed convention is inevit- '
a' -2 because of the lack at this time of sufficient ex
perience with trusts to determine wherein they are
good and wherein they are harmful. Until the true j
nature and the full, extent of the problems are known j
it will not be possible to determine even whether such
evils as exist can be best remedied by State legisla- :
tion or by action of the National Government.
At the recent meeting of the American Bar Asso
ciation Edward Q. Keasbey read a paper on "New !
Jersey and the Great Corporations," in the course of j
which he dealt with some of the evils of the huge .
trusts and combinations which have been lately in- !
corporated in that State. He pointed out that be- I
tween January I and August i of this year there have
been organized in New Jersey alone 1,336 corpora- j
tions having an authorized capital of over two thou- |
sand millions of dollars. After giving a strong ,
presentation of the evil of such combinations, many i
of which arc hardly more than huge gambling !
schemes, Mr. Keasbey virtually admitted that he did i
not know how the evil could be remedied.
The issue is one that must be dealt with in the near
future, but no safe guidance can be expected from an
anti-trust convention called by men who are preju- I
diced at the start and who meet with the avowed ob- j
ject of suppressing trusts altogether. From the Civic '
Federation Conference on the subject, to be held in j
Chicago, much good may be expected, but from the I
St. Louis meeting of a few Governors, most of whom i
are hardly more' than second rate politicians, it is i
not likely that anything will result of benefit to the i
country.

dence that it takes more than a scratch of a pen now- !
adays for even the great powers to convert reluctant i
people to their way of thinking. The Tuaregs be- |
lieve, even if they are savage, that they have one or I
two privileges left.

THE SAW FRANCISCO CALL,, WEDNESDAY, SEPTEMBER 13, 1899.
AN ISSUE OF FACT.
WE recently admonished the Portland Oregonian
that it was wronging itself by imagining vain
things about the Philippine situation. It is a
J very able paper and is not under the necessity of
! doing things in a sm^ll way. It chooses to advocate
imperialism and to submit reasons for our Philippine
policy which have no existence in fact, and to argue
from them as its premise.
Replying, that able paper says that The Call said:
i "The Filipinos had already conquered all of Luzon
! outside of Manila when the Americans appeared.
1 This, even if true, would be no answer to the Ore
| gonian's statement. Bandits, indeed, were largely in
control of the country outside Manila; but Aguin
j aldo, aware that national independence was hope
less, had sold out to the Spaniards for a heavy sum
and left the country, and Spain was increasing her
preparations to crush the bandits when the Americans
appeared."
We must again admonish the Oregonian that mis
icpresentation of what The Call said is an impro
priety that requires an apology, that a gen"r;.l
misrepresentation of the facts is a wrong policy,
which it should not pursue. We did not say that the
Filipinos hjid conquered Luzon outside of Manila
when the American? appeared. The facts, as we
stated them, arc historical, and we quoted one of
' Devvey's officers in their support.
The Filipino insurrection against Spain was ter
minated by a solemn treaty, in which Spain agreed to
ray an indemnity to Aguinaldq of $800,000 and to in
stitute the reforms in administration which the na
tives demanded. In his report to the State Depart
ment our Consul. Wildman, at Hongkong, says:
"There has been a systematic attempt to blacken the
name of Aguinaldo and his Cabinet on account of
the terms of their surrender to the Spanish. It has
been said that they sold their country for gold, but
this is conclusively disproved, not only by their own
1 statements but by the speech of the latt Governor
; General Rivera, in the Spanish Senate, Tune v, 1808.
He said that Aguinaldo undertook to submit if the
Spanish Government would give a certain sum to the
i widows and orphans of the insurgents. Rivera then
admits that only a part of the sum was given to
Aguinaldo, and that he (Rivera) did not find it ex
pedient to keep the other promises made."
Artacho, a follower of Aguinaldo, desired to divide
this money between the native leaders, but it is in
evidence that Aguinaldo refused, saying it was a trust
fund for his country and should be used to renew the
1 insurrection if Spain failed to keep the treaty and
institute the reforms which it promised. She did
fail, as Rivera admitted. The moment armed resis
tance ceased she violated her solemn promise.
Wildman's report continues that he opened nego
tiations with Aguinaldo's Cabinet after consultation
with Admiral Dewey, and that Aguinaldo was in
Singapore, where Consul Pratt was negotiating with
! Admiral Dewey for his return to Luzon. Wildman
i-ent him to Cavite on the McCulloch May 16. 189 S.
Wildman then says: "Immediately on the arrival of
Aguinaldo at Cavite he issued a proclamation, which
I had outlined for him before he left, forbidding pil
lage and making it a criminal offense to maltreat
â  neutrals. He of course organized a government of
which, he .was dictator, an absolutely necessary step if
he hoped to maintain control over the natives, and
from that date until the present time he has been
uninterruptedly successful in the field and dignified
and just at the head of his government. The insur
gents are fighting from freedom from Spanish rule
and rely on the well known sense of justice that con
trols all the actions of our Government as to their
future."
Following events in their chronological order, our
next witness is that ardent imperialist, ex-Minister
John Barrett, appointed to Siam from Oregon. In
I an address at Shanghai, China, last January, Mr. Bar
rett said: "I saw Aguinaldo put aboard a United
States dispatch boat in Hongkong harbor last May,
: by permission of Admiral Dewey and Consul General
i Wildman, for the direct purpose of going to Cavite
\ to organize an army and a temporary government to
; make war on the Spaniards in co-operation with the
i American forces. lie has organized an army out of
â  nothing, which he has developed into a force of 30,000
' men, armed with modern rifles.' He captured all
1 Spanish garrisons on the island of Luzon outside of
Manila, so that when the Americans were ready to
I proceed against the city they were not delayed and
: troubled with a country campaign. Moreover, he has
organized a government which has practically been
: administering the affairs of that great island since
1 the American occupation of Manila, which was cer
; tainly better than the former (Spanish) administra
tion."
One of Dewey's officers, writing from Iloilo, says:
"Aguinaldo reached Cavite May 20. 1808, at which
! time there was no evidence, that we knew of, of x
; native organization. He created an army in a short
[time and immediately commenced to win victories.
j These were to us at the time astonishing, for he de
feated detachment after detachment of the Spanish.
â¢ army, took fort after fort, captured regiments with
i arms and ammunition, and in a few months had cap
j tured every Spanish soldier in the island of Luzon
jor had driven those not captured into Manila. Time
and again I have witnessed whole battalions of Span
' ish soldiers marched as prisoners into San Roquc.
Before August 13. 1808, these insurgents had actually
; captured the whole of Luzon, except Manila and
I Cavite. After taking all of Luzon, with the excep-
I tions named, they conquered the Spanish in the isl
ands of Negros and Cebu, and before we reached Iloilo
I they had this city and the whole island of Panay on
j which it is situated. Besides these there are hundreds
of smaller islands which are under the authority of
Aguinaldo's government at Malolos. More remark
: able to me than Aguinaldo's military actions has been
; the civil organization which he has perfected through
j out these islands, which now governs and rules them
' except in Manila nnd Cavite."
These, in their order, are the facts of history. We
i have named our witnesses and given their testimony.
I From it the fact is incontestably establ'-'hed that
Aguinaldo did not "sell out" to Spain; tha. .iis peo
! pie were not bandits; that he held $400,000 in trust
for his countrymen; that Spain violated her treaty
with him; that Consul Pratt went to seek him in
j Singapore, and. after negotiating with Wildman and
< Dewey. arranged for him to turn back to Cavite; that
Wildman wrote his proclamation by which his gov
! eminent was established and his army raised to co-
I operate against Spain with ours: that "the direct
J purpose" of his return, as arranged with Dewey and
the Consuls, was the organization of the government
which he formed, and that he, with his army, cap
tured, conquered and possessed the four islands of
Luzon, Cebu, Negros and Panay. except Manila and
Cavite, and that the surrender of Spanish sovereignty
over them was to him and not to us, and that under
the laws of nations and the laws of war his govern
ment and not Spain was the sovereign power there
when we bought his people of Spain for two dollars
a head. Yet, the Oregonian says: "In fighting qual
ity and all other qualities they (the Filipinos) are far
below our original North American Indians." But
i Consul General Wildman says officially: "In con
clusion I wish to put myself on record as stating that
the insurgent government of the Philippine Islands
cannot be dealt with as though they were North
American Indians willing to be removed from one
reservation to another at the whim of their masters.
Now will the Oregonian please to call its witnesses
and quote their testimony?
If there is anything in the Dreyfus case that
reaches the sublime it is perhaps the absurdity of
suggesting a boycott upon the Paris Exposition by
the Congress of the United States. While certain
Congressmen by breeding and education have the
unqualified right of making themselves ridiculous,
they should show some toleration for their fellows.
A CHANGE IN RURAL NEW ENGLAND.
SOME years ago the abandoned farms of New
England constituted a topic which engaged the
attention of the whole country. The number
of idle farms was so large in some localities that sev
eral States went to the expense of advertising them
at public cost lor the purpose of attracting settlers
and preventing what threatened to be a widespread
decay of the rural industries of that section of the
Union.
The advertising given to the lands has had its effect
and most of them have been taken up. There are no
longer any great bargains to be had in the way of
abandoned farms, and, according to a late report on
the subject, "One may travel far through even the
remote sections of New England without finding any
large number of farms that may properly be called
deserted. * * * In one way or another the list of
abandoned farms has been greatly reduced, until in
many sections the number is not greater than would
be expected under normal conditions."
The change has not been brought about, however,
by the reoccupation of the farms by a class of genuine
farmers, nor even by people retiring from the cities to
undertake farm work. We are told some have been
bought by wealthy and fashionable people and turned
into more or less elaborate summer places. Others
have been taken up by professional men of limited
means, drawn by the prospect of obtaining a vacation
home at small expense. A good many have passed
into the hands of owners of adjoining property, who
have at last come to see that, other things being equal,
a large farm is likely to be more profitable than a
small one. Some have been acquired for the timber
they bear, and a few have been bought for speculation.
The use of the farms for summer resorts, hunting
grounds, forest reserves and speculation does not on
the face of it give much promise of upbuilding the
mrual industries of a country, but it appears, never
theless, to have had that effect. The persons who have
purchased the farms have had money enough to im
prove them, and therefore have given the neighbor
hood an appearance of thrift and prosperity which has
had the effect of stimulating the residents to improve
their farms also. Moreover, a more scientific system
of farming has been introduced by the new comers,
and the old-fashioned farmers have profited by it.
Labor-saving machinery, scientific tillage, has become
the order of the day, and the value of stock and
orchards has advanced.
Along with all that there lias been a marked change
in the social conditions of rural New England. The
farms are passing into the hands of men who instead
of tilling the soil themselves hire others to do the
work. Thus the rate of fatfn wages is rising, and the
old-fashioned farmer finds it harder than ever to make
a living. Summing up the whole situation, a writer
for the New York Post says: "That the process will
eventually give us a type of alternating town and
country life, akin to that prevailing in England, is by
no means improbable, if, indeed, such a type is not
already established. The resident farmer, on the other
hand, has but two courses open to him if he wishes to
be assured a comfortable living. He may accept the
new conditions, and become himself a landed pro
prietor and agricultural entrepreneur on the new
scale, or he may resign his independence and become
a hired man. For there is going on quietly, in our
day, a social and industrial change likely, before many
years, to give the development of the farms of New-
England into the charge of men who themselves never
swung a scythe or held a plow."
OUR MINERAL PRODUCTION.
OWING to the great variety in the units of
measurement, says the official summary
of the mineral production of the United
States for 1898, the only factor common to all min
eral products and the only means by which the com
parative totals can be expressed is the value.
Taking that common measurement, the statistics
show that since 1880 the increase in the mineral pro
duction of the United States has been from an. output
valued at $369,319,000 to one valued at $697,880,002 in
1898, or nearly 90 per cent.
It is noted that the increase has been very irregu
lar. The average during the nineteen years under
J review has been an increase of $18,000,000 annually,
but from 1894 to 1895 the increase was nearly $100,
--000.000, while in the following year it was but little
more than $1,000,000. In the two years from 1892 to
1894 there was a decrease of over $121,000,000. From
1896 to 1897 the increase was about $8,000,000, while
from 1897 to 1808 it was $66,970,534. The output
of 1898 was the largest in our history, exceeding that
of 1892, the next largest, by nearly $50,000,000.
All the metals made large gains except nickel.
Copper, lead, zinc, aluminum and antimony reached
their maximum both in quantity and in value, but it is
notable that while the amount of pigiron produced
in' 1898 was greater than in any other year, yet the
value of the product was considerably less than in
1890 or 1892, though it increased $21,434,701 over the
value of the output of 1897.
The non-metallic products increased during the
year from $327,710,066 to $352,783,872, a gain of $25,
--072,906. The largest contributor to the gain was
bituminous coal, which increased from $119,567,224 in
1897 to $132,586,313 in 1898. The value of the out
i put of anthracite coal, on the 0 *ier hand, fell off
nearly $4,000,000. The other non-metallic products
showing important gains were stone, petroleum, nat
ural gas and mineral writers.
In the summary given of the output and value of
the precious metals it is noted the gold product con
tinued to increase, and in 1898 was valued at $64,463,
--000, as compared with $57,363,000 in 1897. In 1896
it was $53,088,000. The coining value of the silver
product in 189S was $70,384,485, while the commercial
value was but $32,118,420. In 1897 the coining value
was $60,637,172 and the commercial value $32,316,000.
This is an increase for the year of $747,313 in the
coining value and a decrease of $197,580 in the com
mercial value. The quantity of the production in
creased from 53,860,000 ounces in 1897 to 54.438.000
ounces in 1898. In 1806 the product was 58,834,800
ounces.
