HERE is a coin-moving problem which can be introduced at any gathering
you may attend. A piece of paper with twenty squares marked on it will
do, care being taken to have the heavy marks in the right place. The idea
Is to get the nickels over to the penny side and the pennies to the nickel
side in the fewest moves possible.
Each move forwards two coins of the same kind, but each coin must move but
one space. The barriers, indicated by the heavy lines, cannot be crossed. Start
at either side. The numbers are used to define the spaces. As there are so many
ways of starting and moving, it will not be necessary to explain how your result
was reached. There is, however, a lower limit than some will discover.
UNHAPPY WIVES SEEK
FREEDOM IN DIVORCE
LYMAN I. MOWRY IS CHARGED
WITH CRUELTY.
Mrs. Emma Stierlen Accuses Her
Husband of Unfaithfulness and
Names Rose Scott as Co-
Respondent.
Elizabeth R. Mowry filed suit yesterday
against her husband, Lyraan I. Mowry,
the well-known attorney, for a divorce.
Cruelty la alleged as ground of action.
In her complaint Mrs. Mowry states
that she married her husband November
2, 18S7. As specific instances of cruelty
she alleges that notwithstanding that
she is a singer and has a tender
throat her husband insisted on smok
ing in the house. WVille they were
living at 316 Haight street, at 1719 Oak
and at the Berkshire, she says, he fre
quently remained out late at night, and
when expostulated with would use un
seemly epithets and threaten to kick
down the door. Her suffering was all
mental, she. admits, but sufficient to war
rant her a decree as prayed for.
Mrs. Emma Stierlen, the wife of George
Stterlen, a capitalist well known in this
city and Los Angeles, also filed suit
against her husband for divorce. She
alleges desertion and infidelity, naming
Rose Scott of Los Angeles as co
j respondent. Mrs. Stierlen alleges that her
husband is now living with Rose Scott
! and refuses to give any attention to her
pleas for aid. Hence she asks for a divi
sion of the community property, valued
at about ?50,000, and for $250 a month ali
mony.

AMUSEMENT
IN VARIETY AT
THE ORPHEUM
SINCE the advent of the American
Biograph at the Orpheum about
everybody stays to the end of the
programme, and they all feel glad
they do at the finish. If any particular
subject happens to be dull it is forgotten
in the interest of the others. The pic
tures seem to be the outcome of keen ob
servation and well chosen opportunity.
The attention of the most unappreciative
every-day mind is closely held and re
warded.
Of the new people. White and Harris,
the singing and dancing duo, were par
ticularly interesting to me up to a cer
tain point. The young woman sings
"My Ann Eliza" with such a rush of
spirits and dances with an abandon and
exuberance that leaves me Quite minus a
descriptive adjective. I never saw the
like. She reappears after her first exit
gowned like an inverted French poppy,
is never still a moment and never out of
breath. The other member of the team
puts in a few "falls" with her sixteen
summt-rs to the infinite amusement of the
onlooker, and does other rational things
very cleverly. T'ntil they begin their
burlesque acrobatic work they are ex
cellent. After that their act is just a lit
tle silly, but the orchestra plays so in
cessantly and steadily that you can't
think and you don't realize it until later.
It is like having a tooth extracted with
out pain. "Is he good afterwards?" said
young America at my right when Charley
Case was singing his first song. It was a
brutal question, but since he wasâ and
very goodâ it doesn't matter. He has a
little patent for humor all his ownâ an
after explanation of facts in themselves
simple and without interest. It takes a
deal of quiet thought to make such
things effective, and I don't believe he
has many idle hours. His song about
the press being represented everywhere
might be added to Indefinitely without
danger of wearying.
I wonder if Montrell ever handles
things rationally. I should hate to invite
him to dinner. He might take a notion to
juggle with the glassware and small
plates, and it would be an awful example
fo.r the. children. His spinning firebrands
are more beautiful at second view than
at first and his apparent lack of clever
intention half the battle of his success. It
seems a fitting close to tlie little chil
dren's engagement that they should sing
"Dinah." San Francisco first fell in love
with Arnold at the Tivoli in just that
song and Hazel makes a most effective
Dinah. They are both such. fresh looking
stage children. They seem to have just
wandered out from the cool and quiet of
a nursery and they both look thoroughly
happy. The Crawford sisters are quite
picturesque enough to be interesting, but
they have hardly force or experience
enough to be convincing and they should
not do coon songs. Coon songs are not
for all people. Their jockey dance was
very graceful and very clever and with
singular discretion they put it last, leav
ing a pleasant impression upon the audi
ence. The boys' band plays better this
week than last. Suppe's "Light Cavalry"
and the Strauss waltzes won well-deserv
ed encores. "The Bachelor Club Quartet"
finds favor chiefly through their songs.
They seem to me sort of prophets of a
new day. If some one will take their idea
and build upon it, he will make one of the
very best vaudeville attractions of the
future and find the amusement lover hun
gry for novelty rising up to call him
blessed. Finally, if I could not go to the
Orpheum once a week I should be much
hipped and out of humor.
CHARLOTTE THOMPSON.
SINGULAR CHANGE IN
NATIONAL GUARD LAW
NOVEL INTERPRETATION OS
THE WORD "CONSECUTIVE."
; To Be Out Ninety Bays Is to Be Con
sidered Faithful Consecutive
Service â Resignation Ac
cepted.
General orders No. 8, issued a short time I
since from superior headquarters, of |
which the following is an extract, have i
been the subject of considerable comment
in National Guard circles:
Former members of the National Guard who
have been honorably discharged for "expira- ]
tion of term of service" or on account of |
"removal" and have returned to the limits |
of their commands, and officers who have re
signed, been honorably discharged, or whose I
terms have expired, who re-enlist or re-enter
the guard within ninety days of the date of
their discharge or the expiration of the term
I of office, will be given credit for continuous
| service and the enlistment will be considered
consecutive.
This is an amendment of paragraph 360 [
of "The Rules and Regulations" govern
ing *he National Guard of this State.
Section 1941 of the Political Code says:
The commander In chief is authorized and j
< has power to establish and prescribe such I
rules and regulations, forms and precedents
! not inconsistent with the provisions of the
codes, as he may deem proper for the use,
government and instruction of the National
Guard, and to carry Into full effect the pro- !
visions of the codes relative thereto.
In section 1936 of the same code the- - e
is:
All officers, non-commlssloned officers and |
privates who have faithfully served in the (
| military service of this State for the -space I
' of seven consecutive years, and received the
: certificate of the adjutant general certif'-ing |
the same, are thereafter exempted from further
military and Jury duty except In time of v.-..r.
According to the code it must appear ;
' that there has been seven consecutive i
| years of faithful service before an officer, I
j non-commissioned officer or a private can [
> obtain a certificate of exemption. All tlu>
i authorities agree as to the meaning of !
! the word "consecutive," and the deflni- i
! tloris of that word are: "Following in a |
; train, succeeding one another in regular
order, successive, uninterrupted in course
or succession." The amendment places a
new construction on the word by saying ;
that a break of ninety days, during which
period the individual is in no way con
nected with the guard, is "continuous ser- j
vice," providing, of course, that at any <
time within those ninety days the honora- I
bly discharged individual shall re-enlist or j
n-.-entt-r the guard.
It is claimed that if there is authority j
to grant a leave of ninety days the tims i
can be extended to years. A man once dis
charged from the service of the State sev- ;
ers his connection therewith absolutely j
and the force of this is recognized in gen
eral orders. In which is:
"A man once discharged from the Na- \
tional Guard can become a member again
oniy by enlistment or commission as pro- j
\ :dad by law."
Certain rewards or privileges are often
allow* d in the United States army for re- :
enluiment, but whatever they are Is dis
tinctly stated in the law governing the ac- !
tion. 'such as section 12SU of the Revised j
Statutes of the United States, and other
sections, but there is nothing in the law '
governing the National Guard of Califor- j
n'a that gives power to any one to de- !
clare that any honorably discharged sol
flier who has been out of the guard for â 
ninety days may on the last day of that j
period re-enlist in or re-enter the guard '
and be given credit for continuous service
! from the date of his discharge.
I The purpose of this change is not under- ;
stood, unless it is to give any individual, I
who leels so inclined, an opportunity to !
serve, two or three days after enlistment, :
"remove" and be honorably discharged, j
| and then at the end of ninety days re-en- |

ANSWERS TO CORRESPONDENTS.
SHERMAN AND GRANTâ C. F. S.,
City. The Sherman is a larger vessel than
the Grant as to length and tonnage. The
Grant is a little broader than the Sher
man.
NEVADAâ Subscriber, City. Nevada
was originally a part of Mexico and was
eerled to the United States by the treaty
of Guadalupe Hidalgo, in 1848. It was or
ganized as a separate Territory in 1861
and was admitted as a State of the Union'
October 31, 1864, by the President's proc
lamation, in accordance with an act of
Congress.
LIEU LANDâ J. M. V., Genessee, Cal.
The State is entitled to all the land em
braced within the sixteenth and thirty
sixth sections within its limits. As very
many of these were found to be within
Spanish or Mexican grants which had
been confirmed and were appropriated by
the giantees or their assigns the State has
authorized locations to be made upon
other public lands in lieu of the lands in
the sixteenth and thirty-sixth sections.
TAHICMIPI-W., Ornbaun, Cal. This
department has not been able after an
extensive research to discover any reason
for the change of name of the town in
Kern County from Tahichlpi, the original
Indian name, which means "valley of the
oaks," to Tehachapi. Prior to the rail
road accident, which happened near that
point many years ago, the original name
prevailed, and it was so down in the
guide as the Postofflce name, but after
that accident the name appeared in the
papers as "Tehachapi," and that spelling
has been folio-wed ever since, it even ap
pearing so in the Postal Guide. In the
gazetteers, latest issue, the name is given
as "Tahichipi" or "Tehachapi." If any or
the readers of this department can fur
nish an explanation for the change, tne
same will be published.
PRESS COMMENTS
"Yellow Journalism" of the Journal.
New York Herald.
PARIS, Sept. 2, 1899.
To thÂ« Editor of the Herald: The letter
published in the New York Journal of
August 13 was never written, dictated or
signed by me. HTACIJJTHE
(Pere Hyaclnthe).
It was unlikely that the great French
preacher would ever see a copy of the
Journal in which the alleged communica
tion from him was printed in big type,
with flaring headlines.
But the unexpected happened. Some
friend perhaps mailed him a copy. In
some way it was brought to his notice,
and in an outburst of indignation he wrote
the above note embodied in a special cable
dispatch from Paris to this morning's
Herald.
Recently the journal against which
Pere Hyacinthe now complains published
what purported to be a dispatch from the
eminent Parisian journalist. Francis de
Pressensc. When this gentleman's atten
tion was called to it he publicly, through
the columns of the Aurore, denounced It
as a forgery.
It is quite unnecessary to make any
comment on such facts. They speak for
themselves.

Women Who Lead in Philanthropy
Sacramento Bee.
Among the Abou Ben Adhems that have
arisen among 1 the women in this country
surely Mrs. Leland Stanford and Mrs.
Phebe Heaxst lead all the rest. In ster
ling philanthropy â that philanthropy
which does good with money while the
philanthropist is alive, and does not
hoard all the treasures until death, scat
tering them when the philanthropist could
not possibly do anything elseâ it is doubt
ful if the modern world has seen any
better examples than those furnished by
these two women of California.
What Mrs. Stanford has done at the Le
land Stanford Jr. University has been
rivaled by the magnificence of the dona
tion given by Mrs. Phebe Hearst to the
State University.
Together or separately these women
furnish shining examples of a philan
thropy which is the wonder and tne
admiration of the entire civilized world
to-day.

The Farmers' Vote on the War.
New Whatcom Blade
Farm journals taking votes among
their subscribers in every 3tate in the
Union prove that the Declaration of In
dependence still lives in the United
States. Of all the returns we have seen
quoted the average is at least two to
one in favor of granting independence to
the Filipinos.

MAGNIFICENT INSTITUTION OF LEARNING
THE MARVELING WORLD HAS EVER GAZED UPON
I Plars Replete With
| Rape Jlptistic Beauty |
CALIFORNIA the golden is noth
ing if not magnificent. Her moun
tain?, her plains, her soils and
her climate are all of a nature
that gives inspiration to her hu
manity, ami when a human concept is
-d it, too, must of necessity be on
a plane with the general grandeur of
the commonwealth.
.-iicn magnitude is the conception
of Mrs. Phebe R. Hearst which brought
forth from the best technical minds of
the world the detail and ensemble of
what purp"s>-s t" be the grandest and
most Rrtistic grouping of subsidiary
buildings into one vast institution of
learning that the workl has ever Been.
The story of the contest, which stands
without parallel in the history "f the
world, has been fully set forth in the
papers during the past few weeks, but
all word painting and pictorial repre
sentation of the ideas advanced by
world-famed architects fall far short of
giving a complete understanding of the
magnitude and artistic beauty of the
idealization of California's future uni
versity.
The Call printed a description and
â photographic reproduction of the gen-
eral idea of buildings as represented by j
the winner of the prize in the compe- ,
Ition. but owing to the , imper fee ions
of a hurried photograph the illustra- ]
tion did but meager justice to the great
institution of learning which wHI Â»o ,
b ,g ln to be reared on Berkeley's classic |
hills. In limited space it is impossib.e ,
to do more than show the general idea ,
of the artist who evolved the plans and
Ot n- â  â  Brtty all of the exquisite beaut>
of detail is lost. It is only when the
complete grouping of the plane is seen
that the mind begins to grasp the full
meaning of the competition which Mrs.
Hearst inaugurated.
When the international architectural
jury decid-d. after long study, that
Monsieur E. Benard had pnscnted the i
best and most complete plans for an j
Ideal university it waa not a judgment :
I alone upon the totality of the
ption, but was also a tribute to
the artistic attention to beauty of de
tail combined with a most practical de
sign in grouping of the various build- j
hi^fl which go to make the grand whole, j
The various edifices which have been
planned are not only designed with the
most complete attention to the require
ments of the especial study, but the
! ornamentation is peculiarly adapted to
. the particular branch <>f learning for
; which it is to be the home.
The great nave of the ferry depot
was thronged last night with the
alumni of the university who had been
Invited to m^et Messrs. Pascal, Wal
lot, Belcher and Cook, the jury which
has spent many months in deciding
whi<*h of the many plans submitted
was the one best suited for the require
ments. While hundreds saw the plans
thousands were unable to attend, and
The ('all reproduces from photographs
;by The Call's special photographer
P'ime of the most artistic bits of the
future institution of learning which
will make California world renowned.
Mrs. Hearst, whose illness prevented
1 her presence, had joined the regents
! with a special invitation to those who
! have in the past profited by the uni
j versity to inspect the designs for the
' future seat of learning and become ac
! quainted with the men who have made
the selection out of the many present
ed, and it was expected that at this
meeting she would make the announce
ment of her intention to begin the ma
terialization of the scheme which Is
â¢now in the realms of the ideal.
It has been known for some time that
Mrs. Hearst contemplated the erection
of one or more buildings for the univer
sity, but it is now made public for the
first time that this desire on her part
was the inception of the idea to have a
competition of architects to design a,
magnificent university. While she
Wished to erect a building she wanted
that building to be in harmony with the
future growth of the university. Her
selection is to be erected as a memorial
to her husband, and as he was a min
ing man her choice was naturally the
mining building. This she will begin
the erection of immediately after the
acceptance of the plans by the regents,
and following closely upon this she
will erect another as a reminder of
herself. While her selection on this
point has not yet been made public, It
is understood that it will be the
women's building.
1 It is announced that Mayor James D.
Phelan and Miss Jennie Flood will each
erect a building, and it is expected that
several other of the wealthy alumni of
the institution will show their apprecia
tion of the possibilities of the future by
assisting in the consummation of the
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, SEPTEMBER 13, 1899.
present ideal; and while the magnitude!
of the conception is startling at first it j
is among the probabilities that the
present generation will see what is now
apparently but a dream become a beau
teous reality.
The plans will be on exhibition at the
ferry depot until next Saturday, when
they will be formally submitted to the
regents of the university; and should
they be accepted, of which there seems
no reason to doubt, the progress from
idealization to realization of the project
will be a mere question of time and in
clination of those who are most imme
diately interested in the future of this
great State.
Millions of dollars will be required to
bring the matter to a conclusion and
years of time will elapse before the
magnificent adornment of the Berkeley
hills is complete, but from the day
that word went, over the world that
California wanted designs for the most
complete institution of learning on the
globe, the fame of the State and the
munificence of Mrs. Phebe Hearst, who
made the idea a possibility, became
world wide. The jury of architects
have served faithfully and well and
their labors have given to California,
as much as those of the successful de
signer, the plans of a university that
the world itself will be proud of.
The labors of the jury are over and
the members will soon return to their
respective homes, undoubtedly carry-
Ing with them memories of the broad
hospitality of the State which called
them together. They have been feast
ed and feted by a grateful people who
have found time amid the rejoicing
over returning sons to give honor to
those who have done so much for the
future of the youth of the common
wealth.
Prior to the reception last evening
the gentlemen composing the jury were
given a dinner at the Cliff House, and
as they listened to the roar of thÂ«
waves as the surf dashed on the rocks
Â§JI World Lays Tribute $
I at California's Feet Â£
a
they might -well have felt that thay
had completed the inception of a
mental achievement which not all the
natural grandeur of the world could
enshadow or eclipse.
ARREARS OF WORK BY
THE BOARD OF REGENTS
The Regents of the University of Cali
fornia met in regular session yesterday
afternoon, General Barnes presiding.
Nothing but routine business was trans
acted, the passing of bills and acting upon
recommendations from the various com
mittees.
Just before the meeting adjourned Re
gent Budd called for a vote upon th
amendment of article 365 of th
by-laws, a change which he had propoaa
I two months before and which would make
! the regular meeting days of the Regents
; the first Tuesdays in September. Decem
-1 ber. March and June and the day before
commencement. Regent Wallace objected
i to the amendment in strong and vigorous
language, contending that the university's
needs demanded that the Regents meet at
least once a month; but the majority of
! the Regents present voted for the amend
! ment.
Kx-Presiderit Kellogg, who Is now away
| on a vacation, asked the Regents for a
leave of absence and a continuance of his
salary. Regent Hallidie championed Pro
fessor Kellogg's cause, but Regent Foote
opposed it. The matter was laid on the
table awaiting the arrival of President
Wheeler.
New changes will be .made in the gym
i nasium, which will be enlarged so as to
serve as an auditorium witn a capacity of
1200 seats. This will cost $2000. An addition
will be made to the conservatory, and a
new laboratory will be added to the chem
ical building, the improvements aggregat
i ing $10,000. ,
Considerable discussion was indulged in
over the medal fund. At present the cost
of medals Cor the highest graduating
scholar is $125. but the annual income from
the fund reaches $237 60. It being a trust
fund, the Regents were in a quandary
what to do with the surplus, and as the
matter involved some legal technicalities
it was put upon the table for future con
sideration.
Professor Rising was allowed an assist
ant at $300 a year; J. A. Rowell. library
assistant at $40 per month: J. A. de Con,
assistant in Latin, $10 per month: W. H.
j Alexander, reader in Latin at $10 per

Dried Fruit in New York.
â  York Commercial says:
"The increased interest In dried fruits con
tinues, and negotiations are in progress look-
Ins toward heavy Bales In the near future..
There is no tfiiotable change in prices, but there
ib a firmer feeling on everything except
peaches. Dealers begin to expect a revival of
business, which will partially compensate for
the dullness that has characterized trade fur
the past few weeks.
"The prune situation is improving and prices
on Santa Claras tend upward, though so far
as known no actual advance has occurred,
compared with opening prices. The decision
of the growers not to sell below a specified fig
ure holds prices at about the opening price. It
is a fact, however, that some sales have been
made as low as 2%c for outside goods, and con
siderable blocks are reported to have gone out
at 2%C Santa Claras are held firmly at 3c. j
Growers Insist upon receiving their prices, bas
ing their demands upon a reduced output In
tliis country, compared with last year, and a
shortage abroad. Movement is expected to im
prove from now on till the bulk of the crop la
disposed of.
"Raisins on the spot axe firmer, and three
crowns are up He. The old goods have been
cleant-d up by sales to Western commission
houses and a few in Eastern markets. No
prices had bÂ«#en made on new goods yesterday,
and It is said that none were expected before
September 20. It is thought that prices havo
been pretty accurately foreshadowed, and that
the stocks most in demand will sell not far from
the price given. The market will be in a very
favorable position. Last year at the opening
of the season there were fully 1000 cars
of carry-over goods. This year the association
has disposed of all its holdings, and dealers are :
not heavily stocked. As the market is guar- |
anteed against decline, there will be little
chance for cut rates. It would appear.
"Peaches are the only variety of dried fruit
which are neglected. No one wants to buy
under present conditions, and holders are
forced to urge sales to do much from first
hands. It is said that peaches have been sold
In the interior of California for 4%c, but the
ranj;e generally quued Is s%'Â§'6c. A few sell
at fi%p, but not many. There Is no uniform
system of grading, anil choice means widely
varying qualities In different growing sections.
"Apricots are nearly all out of first hands.
and i-rices re held firm on all grades. Trading
Is small at present."

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
The pressure has risen rapidly over the
northern half of the Pacific Coast. An area
of low pressure overlies Arizona and South
eastern California. The temperature has fallen
I'j degrees or more over Oregon and Southern
Washington. It has risen about 6 degrees over
Utah. In the great valleys of California the
temperatures are still above the Yiormal. Cloudy
weather prevails along the northern coast of
California and showers have occurred from the
north of the Columbia River north and east.
A thunderstorm is reported at Raker City; a
maximum wind velocity of 28 miles per hour
fn.m the south at Pocatelio. At San Francisco
there is a vertical thermal gradient of 1 degree
rise to 100 ieet of elevation.

Chicago Grain Market.
CHICAGO. Kept. 12 -At the Immediate opon
inp in wheat yesterday's bullish Government
j crop report did not have any appreciable effect
on pries. On the contrary the market was in
clined toward weakness, the bears taking for
their cue the he.avy Northwest receipts, offering
wheat in considerable quantities. The result,
was small declines in everything. It was evl-
I dent that the Governrm-nt report, which mdii
i cattd a total crop of something like 487,000,
--| 000 bushels, was not so lightly regarded in the
j country, for outside buying orders beuan to
stream in shortly after the opening. The buy
ing was so brisk in comparison with the recent
dullness that for the time being it took the
; breath away from the professional bears and
prices shot upward with considerable rapidity.
December opened a shade lower at 71^7ivic
nnd reached 71% c. before realizing became heavy
: enough to nnnsr a temporary setback. The
tone Of the flixir gradually became decidedly
bullish, the sentiment, being strengthened by
outside buflnt. and shorts covered freely.
! Prices kept on the up grade until within thirty
; minutes of the close, with the broadest business
lln weeks. December advanced to 71 7 ,fc<3>72c.
This was well above call figures, hut no re
action of importance took place until the post
ing of Uradstreefs llgures on the world's visi
ble, Bhowlng an increase of 4.935,000 bushels.
This was far beyond what the trade antici;>at>'<l
and had an immediate effect. In the meantime
shorts had covered generally and the selling
! movement which started found little opposi
tion. Prices dropped rapidly from that time
to the close, though September was given bet
! ter support than the more distant futures. De
i cember w.-Â«s the object of a determined raid by
professional bears, and at the close all of the
! earlier advance had disappeared. December de
clined to 71@T1V, and was bringing that price
at the close. September closed with a gain of
at 70% c.
Corn was fairly active and irresrular. The
market early felt the Government report, con
siderable outside buying being done, especially
In deferred futures. Later in the day the mar
ket became weak and all the advance was lost.
December closed a shade higher and Septem
ber a shade lower.
Oats were rlrm and higher, though not
maintaining best prices. The market was
steady at the close. December closed with a
gain of %c to y*c.
Provisions were weak all day and closed at
the bottom of a substantial decline. Hog
prices were lower and yellow fever news waa
disquieting. The strength of grain markets
probably prevented a larger decline. At the
close October pork was 10 cents lower, lard 5
cents lower and rlhs 5 to 7>4 cents lower.

) Butter, Cheese and Eggs.
Butter is off again. Some sales are still
made at 26c, but close buyers no longer pay It.
The recent advance diverted a good deal of
the demand to pickled goods, and It has
stayed there. Cheese is steady and unchanged.
Eggs are quiet and rather weak at the de
cline already noted.

THE STOCK MARKET.
There was a moderate business in securities
on the morning session at unchanged quota
tions.
In the afternoon Giant Powder advanced to
Mining stocks weakened oft considerably, as
will be seen by the list of sales. The telegram
from the pump said: "No. 1 elevator has been
running continuously. The water is 34 feet
below the 1950-foot level station. To-day we
begin work preparatory to taking out elevator
No. 2 for rearrangement."
The Texas Gold Mining Company of Willow
Valley. Nevada County, has levied an assess
ment of 5 cents per share, delinquent Octo
ber 31.
A dividend of 20 cents per share, payable on
Uip l.'t h. has been declared by the Oakland
Shu Leandro and Haywanln Railroad Company!
The monthly dividend of 7n cents per share
will be paid by the Alaska Packers' Associa
tion to-day.
The California Street Cable Company will
pay a monthly dividend of 50 cents per shar.?
to-day.
The Gas Consumers' Association paid the
usual dividend this month
The Calumet and Hecla Copper Company of
Michigan has declared a dividend of $20 per
share, the second of like amount for the pres
ent fiscal year, beginning May 1. The com
pany last March paid a dividend of $20 per
share and $20 per share extra, the last named
from reserve accumulated in some years, the
two aggregating $4,000,000, the largest mining
dividend ever paid In this country. The divi
dend Inst May was $2,000,000, same as the one.
Just declared, which is payable on September
29. This is the one hundred and twelfth divi
dend and makes a total of $64,850,000. the larg
est sum to the credit of any mining company
in the United States.
The Gwln Mine Development Company has
declared its tenth dividend at the rate of 5
cents per share, pnyable now. The total divi
dends amount to $Sfi,sWi.
The Gould and Curry Mining Company re
ports $1239 38 cash on hand September 1. with
an indebtedness at bank of tv<Oo and 1. â 
lecting an assessment. ' and v co 1"

United States Coast and Geodetic Survey-
Times and Heights of High and Low
Waters at Fort Point, entrance to San
Francisco Bay. Published by official au
thority of the Superintendent.
NOTEâ The high and low waters occur at
the city front (Mission-street wharf) about
twenty-five minutes later than at Fort Point;
the height of tide is the same at both places.
' WEDNESDAY, SEPTEMBER 13.
NOTEâ In the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given In the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day in the order of occurrence as to time. The
second time column gives the second tide of
the day, the third time column the third tide
end the la.Â«t or right hand column gives the
!ast tidÂ£ of the day, except when there are but
three tides, as sometimes occur. The heights
p;i\en are addition to the soundings on the
I'nlted States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign (â ) precedes the height,
and then th" number given Is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference is the mean of the lower low waters.

DEFEATED BY THE PEOPLE
Judge Troutt Enters a Second
Judgment Against the
Corporation.
Its Efforts to Evade the Payment of the War
Revenue and Shift the Burden by Virtue
of a Court Judgment Without Avail.
FOR a second time Judge Treat* has
passed adverse Judgment on Wells.
Fareo & Co. for its refusal to bear
the revenue burden Imposed upon It
by the acts of Congress. Since the
dn&ctment of the war revenue law Wells,
Farg:- &. Co. have stubbornly opposed all
efforts to compel the corporation to obey
the law and Itself furnish and bear the
expense cf revenue stamps required to be
affixed to each bill cf lading or other
manifest issued as a memorandum of re
ceipt. In each legal battle before the
lower courts it has been defeated, but
again marshaled ita forces and "def-nd
e<i" actions brought to compel compliance
with the law. Yesterday, however. Judge
Troutt handed down an opinion from
which an appeal will be taken and final
Judgment passed by the Supreme Court.
This judgment on record. Attorney flan
eral Ford stands ready to enforce It, and
for each violation of its holdings the cor
poration must defend Itself In court If not
In an action brought to compel the forfeit
ure of its franchise.
The action decided yesterday was that
of the people against the corporation on
the relationship of the Assistant Attorney
General, in which the court was prayed to
issue a peremptory writ of mandate
against the company, compelling it to af
f.x. and bear the expense of revenue
stamps as required by the war expendi
tures act. Attorney General Ford pros
ecuted the case of the people, and his ar
gument left practicaliy out one hole.
through which the company sought to
crawl tn its effort to beat the people and
the Government, to which it owes its ex
istence. The corporation lawyers con
tended that inasmuch as the act in ques
tion dM not specifically state whether or
not the company or the consignor should
bear the burden cf the revenue law as re
lates to the afnxing of stamps to bills of
iading or other manifests, the matter
should fee left to the parties in interest,
and the matter of affixing the stamp, and
its attendant expense, left to agreement
reached by argument or barter between
the shipper and the carrier. While thus
presenting its case the company neglected
to mention its superior power over the or
dinary consignor, but the court doubtless
considered that i- left to agreement be
tween the consignor and the corporation
the latter would devise ways ana means
by which to escape the burden. The money
would be raised and the purpose of the
law subserved, but. as before, the people
would again be the carrier of a shifted
burden. Hence the ccurt did not consider
the defense of the company a sufficient
one. and on motion of the Attorney Gen
eral has entered judgment In favor of the
people and against the corporation on the
pleadings.

SOUTHERN STREAMS
STOCKED WITH TROUT
Young Fish Taken From the State
Hatchery and Are Doing
Well.
PASADENA. Sept. 12.â Game Warden
Charles Winston said to-day that 25,000
ralr.bow trout have been placed in moun
-1 tain streams hereabouts and are doing
well. The fish were hatched at the State
] hatchery In the north and sent her<Â» in
! cars. They are turned loose In creek 3
and cared for by three deputies, who pre
vent peaching. The fishing season has
been closed ever since the mountain fires
of two or three years ago. T^ast leason
! was very dry and many fish died. To
s day the streams are almost depopulated
or native fish. In many places the Game
Warden deputies have had to supply wa
ter to keep the fish alive.
The trout are about two Inches long
wKen they arrive and iron and propagate
very rapidly. Next year fishermen may
have much sport- Until a few years ago
the San Gabriel River was a famous trout
| stream and it is thought that its reputa
tion can be restored.

HIS FUME OF

AT SEVENTY
Hodge-Nicholl Trial
Begun.
Oak' and Office San Francisco Call.
908 Broadway. Sept. 12.
The trial of Miss Jane Hodge's $50,000
breach of promise damage suit against
her aged uncle by affinity. John Nicholl,
the rich East Oakland capitalist, was be
gun in Judge Ogdec's court this after
noon.
Little time was consumed In selecting a
Jury. Among those challenged was A.
Bernal. -who vouched that he "could not
impartially try a case where a woman
sues for breach of promise, because." as i
he put It. "the woman surely ought to be
willing to take even chances with the !
man." The following jury was accepted
and sworn: Chris A. Mulvey, C. B. Har
mon. O. C. Hyatt, M. K. Houghton. Chris
Christensen. Richard Feige. B. F. Maloon.
Conrad Reigelhuth. J. H. Fensher. C. J.
Zobei. W. B." Bridge and Charles F.
Harper.
Miss Hodge, the spinster plaintiff, who
has herself passed fifty summers, was
present with her attorneys, W. W. Foots
and Reed & Nusbaumer. She was gowned
in black, and her white tresses, her Quiet
demeanor as she gazed through her gold
rimmed spectacles, precluded even the j
slightest excuse for any hint that an ad
venturess was scheming to mulct an aged
capitalist of 50.000 glittering dollars for
her lacerated affections.
The defendant. John Nicholl. who has
but recently passed his seventy-eighth
birthday anniversary. was also on hand
with his attorneys, Major John B. Mhoon
and Sam Bell Mcl^ee. But for the perfect
white "galloways" issuing from beneath
his chin, he would pass for a man of 30
years. His face is massive-featured and j
flushed with apparent health, not at ail
Indicative of blood that lags in its clr- j
culation or has cooled of its animal heat. |
Mazie and Heasifl Nlcholls, defendant's i
two daughters, who are saW by Miss
Hodge to have caused the engagement to J
be broken, were also in court, but were j
excluded while illss Hodge testified, J
which she did after Attorney Foote had j
made i brief though comprehensive open
ing statement to the jury.
Miss Hodge first related her advent in
California in 1554, and In Oakland in ". â¢â¢'
She- had frequently met Mr. Nicholl. and j
their relations had always been pleas- |
ant. On November 9, 1888, Mr. Nicholl
had spoken to her about her need of a j
life companion. The act day they had
gone riding, when the aged capitalist
popped the question.
â¢â¢He said." continued the witness, "that
he was going to marry me, and I told i
him I was willing. He told me to go to a â

wanted it. Yes, and he embraced me j
passionately. I afterward told Mazie

ber 10 to December 13 he called on me
nearly every day. Then he ceased, and j
on December 2>> I wrote him a letter .and j

further In our little matter have now de

folks here are beginning to wonder what
we mean to do. Is it not beat that we
show them that we are both in earnest
as to our marriage?" The letter was
signed "Lovingly your promised wife,
Jane Hodge." , .

Lester avenue. East Oakland. Jan. T, 13M.
My Dear Mr. Nicboll: Have Just received
your letter. Am very sorry that you have al

daughter to write such a letter to me. Only

manner. That you love me still I know in
spite of lawyers sad daughters, and will yet
do me Justice.
You know tn your heart of hearts that the
assertion you make In your letter is untrue.
You are an honorable man. I still believe, and
you know that no honorable man could so
plainly show for years his passionate love for
a woman and then ruthlessly throw her aside
In this manner. When you asked me the ques
tion you would have a3ked me years before you

God to bless our union, and when I tearfully
said "You will be good to ie, I know." you
answered "God knows I will." I cannot allow
mysel' tr> break my sacred promise to you, and
I pray God to make you true to yourself and to
Him in keeping yours to me.
If you allow any meddilng lawyers, laugh

greed of gain and Jealousy, you are not the

merel? said. "Say nothing for a few days and
everything will come out all right." I have
allowed no one to dictate to m* what I shall

Pope's Health Is Grocd.
ROME. Sept. 12.â Alarming rumors are
-t that the Pope is ill. but they are
ieclared to be without foundation.
late. For five months there have been lit
tle family differences In the Council, and
or. one memorable occasion some mem
bers accused other members of taking
snap Judgments ' and a retort was made
that "grumbling and growling" would not
help to fix the tax levy. It Is also on rec
ord that one member said he was able to
look out for himself, while another one
accused still another member of acting In
a cowardly manner.
All these little affairs, however, are
simply amicable methods of adjusting
family differences. Mr. Rowe said ao
officially.
Taking snap judgments, making little
threats about self-preservation ana other
little affairs, which have caused mem
bers to turn white or red in the face, do
not indicate hatred, malice, envy or un
charltableness; they are simply Oakland
esque. So say 3 President Rowe. and as
he has presided over all the delibera
tions of the City Council since it took
office he is competent to speak.
When Mr. Mott hints at silurianlsm in
regard to the opening of Washington
street, and suj?gests that some of his col
leagues are guided by sentiment more
than sense: when Mr. Cnrellier says that
some of the members are falling
on the municipal water plant proposition
and breaks his glasses as he says so:
when Mr. Stetson charges Girard with
ecaose Girard chides Stetson
for wasting the time of the Council in
trying to raise a relative's salary: when
Mr Row*'? feelings become so intense
that he does his talking without leaving
the chair; when Mr. Girard hints that
certain people have gone crazy over the
school, when all these or similar
things were done by the old Council a
name was readily found for them. But
now they are merely "friendly discus
sions" carried on in an amicable way as
at "a family gathering." Bo says M-
Rowe, and he also says that the news
papers are all wrong if they place dif
ferent constructions upon them. I

FERRY SUICIDE WASHED
ASHORE AT SAN PABLO
Oakland Office San Francisco Call.
908 Broadway, Sept. 12.
There is no longer any mystery attached
to the identity of the young man who
jumped from the ferry boat on the even
ing of August 21. He was Andrew G.
Amland. residing at 1 Morrow place, San
Francisco.
Yesterday afternoon a body was washed
ashore north of San Pablo. It had been
in the water so long that identification
was impossible, but in the clothing was
found a card bearing the name and ad
dress given. Coroner Curry took charge
of the body and is communicating with
the deceased's relatives in San Francisco.
The floating ashore of the remains of
young Amland bears out the truth fti his
brother's surmise the day after trie sui
cide was reported. When young Amland
lumped from the steamer Oakland the
ferry boat's crew attempt to rescue
him but only succeeded in finding his
hat ' Am!and's brother Charles, fearing
that Andrew had taken his life, carefully
inspected the hat and declared it to be
that of Andrew. : V"Â»; â  Â.
The identification of the suicide also
clears up a romantic story which orig
inated with MrÂ». Clarence Avery, living
on the San Leandro road. Mrs. Avery
was on the boat from which Amland
jumped, and as her husband had left her
a week previously to go East the story
was circulated that the suicide might be
Mr. Avery.

A Soldier's Funeral
ALAMEDA. Sept. 12.â The funeral of
Corporal Hans Jacobson was held from.
Armory Hall at 1 o'clock to-day. The re
ception room, where the body lay in state
under a guard of honor, was decorated.
Chaplain Scott. U. S. A., conducted the
services, his eulogy of the dead soldier
being- very impressive. The pall-bearers
consisted of two men each from Com
panies A. F and G. At the grave in
Mountain View Cemetery the military
burial service was read, three volleys
were fired over the grave and taps
sounded.
Many Odd Fellow Soldiers.
Last evening 125 Odd Fellow soldiers
from all the regiments now at the Presidio
were the guests of Bay City Lodge, I. O.
O. F.. In the Memorial Hail of the OdÂ«l
Fellows' building. There were present
about 250 members of the order to greet
the soldiers. There was an address of
welcome by O. I. Wise and after addresses
by prominent members of the order there
was served a collation.
Tried to End Her Life,
A prepossessing young' woman, who
gave her name as Ella Evans and her
residence at 413 Sanchez street, swallowed
half a bottle of chloroform last nijfhi: In
Jefferson Square. The woman was taken
to the Receiving- Hospital and the usual
antidotes applied. Her desire to end her
existence was due to a lover's quarreL
The doctors think that she will recover.

NOTICE of meeting of stockholders of The Ris-
don Iron and Locomotive Works, to take into
consideration i.roposed increase of capital
stock.
Notice Is hereby given that pursuant â to a
resolution and order of the board of directors
of The Risdon Iron and Locomotive Works,, a
corporation organized and existing under and
by virtue of the laws of the State of Califor-
nia, and having its office and principal place
of business at the city and county of San
Francisco, in said State, unanimously adopted
at a regular meeting of said board held on
the seventh day of August, in the year one
thousand eight hundred and ninety-nine, a
meeting of the stockholders of said corpora- ;
tion will be held on Wednesday, the Eight-
eenth Day of October, in the year one thou-
sand eight hundred and ninety-nine, at thÂ»
hour of two o'clock in the afternoon of said
day, at the office of said corporation, which
Is at the building situate on the easterly cor-
ner of Beale and Howard streets. in said city
and county of San Francisco, the game being
the principal place of business of said corpo-
ration, and being the building where the
board of directors of said corporation usually
meets;
That the object of said meeting Is to deter-
mine whether or not the capital stock of said
corporation shall be increased from one million
dollars, divided Into ten thousand shares of
one hundred dollars each, to a capital stock
of one million one hundred thousand dollar?,
divided into eleven thousand shares of one
hundred dollars each:
That the amount to which it Is proposed to
Increase the said capital stock Is one million
one hundred thousand dollars.
By order of the board of directors.. ,
L. R. MEAD.
Secretary of The Risdon Iron and Locomotive
Works.
Dated August 8. 1?99.

MURDERER HUNT
PROVES TO BE
A DEGENERATE
insane Behavior at
the Inquest.
Robert L. Hunt laughed heartily yes
terday forenoon when witnesses at the
Coroner's inquest described how he shot
and killed George Elliott and seriously
wou:. -keeper E. R. Knippen
berg on Steuart street last. Saturday.
Hunt sat in a corner of the room near
the witness stand and "enjoyed" the pro
ceedings as thqugh they were a farce got
up for his amusement. Several times he
assisted the memory of the witnesses
when they halted in their recital of the
threats made by himself, and was Quick
to prompt them, notwithstanding" the pro
tests of Coroner Hill. Thus, when one of
the witnesses was telling about Hunt hav
ing threatened to murder Mr. Simpson,
Hunt corrected him by giving the initials
as well as the name "A. M. Simpson."
When Hunt was asked whether he
wished to take the stand in his own behalf ;
he said that he did, and took the witness'
chair with cheerful alacrity as though
he were going to tell a funny story. He
declared unhesitatingly that he had done
the shooting and that the soul of Mrs.
Emily Russell had entered into possession
of him and had prompted him to do the
deed. He said that she was known as
Mrs. Delamore and that she had in his
company visited saloons up north and
drank champagne with him. He vus
proud of being obsessed by "the h>ly
spirit of the woman," as he called It, end
did not for an Instant exhibit remorse or
shame. It became apparent that Hunt
was a degenerate and that that fact bad
more to do with his insanity than s'ny
Injury that he might have received upon
the hoad by the fall of a portion of a roof
upon him several years ago, as stated by
his friends. .
Coroner Hill, suspecting this to be the
case, put a question to test Hunt on this
point, and Hunt Immediately became so
obscene in word and action that the ma
tron fled from the room in horror and the !
policeman In charge was obliged to use
force to make the murderer desist. The
Jury returned a verdict charging Hunt
with the murder of Elliott.
Hunt was arraigned in Judge Mogan's
court in the morning on the charge of
assault to murder the saloon-keeper at
1122 euart street and the case was con
tinued until Friday.

ROSENGERE'S DEMAND
Makes Another Application for a
Complaint Against the Union
Iron Works.
Secretary Rosenberg of the Foderate-1
rradi s Council oa-Ut-d yesterday upon As
sistant United States Attorney Banning
SEARCHING FOR THE DERELICT LOG RAFT.
and renewed his wemanci ror a compiami
itnd a warrant for the arrest of the man
agers of the Union Iron on a
! charge of violating the Federal eight-hour
I law by working more than eight hours per
day upon the Government transports get
ting them ready to carry soldiers and
supplies to Manila. Banning asked
I whether Rosenberg was -willing to swear
that the law had been violated since Ban
ning had written to the Union Iron Works
people with regard to the subject, and
whether he would swear that the work
was not one of emergency.
It appeared to Banning that the fitting
out of transports in time of w;ir *was a
matter of emergency, and that being the
case a prosecution would not hold.
Rosenberg left after promising to maite
the necessary investigation.
INTEREST IN LOCAL
POLITICS REVIVING
REPUBLICANS TALK OF SIDNEY
M. SMITH FOR MAYOR.
W. G. Doane Would Not Decline the
Nomination for Auditor â Demo
crats Are on Still Hunt for
Supervisors.
Among delegates to the Republican local
convention there is a good deal of talk
in favor of nominating for Mayor Sidney
M. Smith, president of the Cutting Paok-
Ing Company. Delegates who are advo
g his election assert that his ability
is unquestioned, that his character is
without a blemish and that his knowledge
of San Francisco affairs cannot be dis
puted.
Night before last the Thirty-ninth As
sembly District delegation of the Repub
lican convention held a meeting. The
â  tfs had the pleasure of listening
to a lalk from W. <~>. Doane, who would
not decline a nomination for Auditor. In
order to exercise Influence in the affairs
of the convention the delegates represent
ing the Thirty-ninth will endeavor to vote
as a unit.
This evening the committee of perma
nent organization and order of business
of the convention will meet at the Grand
Hotel. Friday next there will be a meet-
Ing of the executive committee of the
City Republican League. The chairman
of the committee, Sheldon (i. Kellogg,
Will probably receive a hint from th<> ex
t-cutive committee as to the proper time
for calling another session of the nom
inating convention.
Seven del< gates to the Democratic local
convention are still hunting for accept
able names to place on the ticket for
the Board of Supervisors.
The committee of seven met yesterday
in the law office of a. vv. Thompson. The
next session may take place- in Mr Whit
ings office. The Democrats who are anx
ious to get on the ticket can hardly keep
track of the wandering seven. A long lint
of names has been submitted to ti om
mittee, as each district was Invited to rec
ommend candidates, but only eighteen
names will bo presented to the convention.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY; SEPTEMBER 13, 1899.
EXAMINER HAS
TO TAKE ITS
OWN MEDICINE
Crawfishes on One of
Its Fakes.
APOLOGIZES TO OCCIDENTALS
SAYS THE LARKSPUR "ORGY"
WAS A PIPE DREAML
The Author of the Zolaesqne Fairy
Tale Hauled Over the Coals for
His Full-Page "Inadver
tence."
Persons picking up the Examiner this
morning and finding little news will
please excuse that paper. It hadn't time
to get any yesterday as it was so busy
apologizing to the people of Larkspur
for its attack on their characters and so
frantically endeavoring to take back all
it had published concerning the alleged
indulgence of Occidental campers in "bac
chanalian revels."
It was all an "inadvertence," says the
Examiner in explanation of its Zolaesque
portrayal of saturnalia that refused to
stay nailed. Of course the people of
Larkspur believe that statement. Look-
Ing through a pair of Examiner specta
cles they can readily see how an inad
vertence can be spread all over a page.
With the same eyeglasses they can con
jure up a camel doing a flipllap as he
passes through the eye of a needle. It's
all in the point of view, as some phil
osopher has said; all in the point of view.
Sunday morning last, viewed by F.
Bailey Millard. who had an ax to grind
and no water with which to grind it, the
point of view was different. He took up
almost a page of the now tearful Ex
aminer in telling what a bad lot were the
young men of Occidental camp. "Bac- '
chanalian satyrs," "Neroites of Baltl
more Canyon," "hoodlums rampant," j
etc., ad lib", were added to his book of i
synonyms as equivalents for the unsus
pecting campers, lie called their brand- I
new piano a hurdy-gurdy and a dilapi
dated music machine; he said their Dark
town <litties were ragtime dlableries;
andâ most wonderful of all in that
"truthful" descriptionâ he said that they i
swam in the "altogether" while enjoying
siestas!
As the Sunday editor of the Examiner
alone Millard was not satisfied to vent I
bifl spite on the campers. He unloaded
several columns on their devoted heads in
that capacity, but that was not enough.
He lowered himself on the newspaper lad
derâstooped to conquer, as it wereâ and.
ai "a reporter for the Examiner who vis- |
ited the scene yesterday," lashed into
I them for half a column. Even then his,
rage was at the boiling point and It was
i not until he had ripped the revelers up the
\ back as a "cottager" and ripped them
'â  down again as "a resident of Baltimore
> ctnyon who lives on the hillside just
above the camp grounds," that he stopped
I the as the Examiner calls Its "mad
I vc-rttnce."
I On numerous occasions during the week
! preceding the publication of the "lnadver
! tencc." Millard gloated publicly on the
i "package he was about to hand" the
! campers. He boasted to several employes
! of the North Pacific Coast Railroad Com
! pany that he had "a roast prepared that
| would peel the skin from the campers,"
I advising those to whom he spoke to watch
| for the following Sunday edition of the
Examiner.
Mr. Mlllard was busy explaining yester
day to the managing editor how the "In
advertence" ha/i been allowed to take on
| such large proportions. At a late hour
; last night the returns were not all in.
! The managing editor, however, was rest
| Ing somewhat easier than lie did the night
before while supervising the building of a
I retraction that might retract as little aa
< possible In his inside pocket the manag- ;
j ing e'litor of the Kxaminer had the letter
I written by Mlllard to Will Moore, business
manager of the Monitor and manager of
i iccldental camp. Moore "inadvertently"
loaned it to the managing editor, who in
timated he needed it in the business of
animadverting the Mlllard "inadvert
<â¢]](<*." Yesterday afternoon Mr. Moorp,
repenting the fact that he had given the
lotter to the managing editor, tried to get
it again. He was unable to see the man
aging editor, but was told by one of his
representatives that they wished to send
it to Mr. Hearst.
In writing the letter threatening the Oc
cidental campers with all sorts of terms
in Kan Quentin for their alleged felonies,
Mr. Milard had "inadvertently" used an
Examiner letterhead.
Twice again last night a representative
of Mr. Moore tried to secure an audience
with the. managing editor of the Exam
iner, so as to come at the letter, but each
tkne was informed that that functionary
was out. Mr. Moore begins to think that
not the managing editor but he himself
is outâ that letter.
Foster Assevedo, the lessee of the ground
upyn which the fake bacchanalian orgies
were alleged to have taken place, was In
this city yesterday in consultation with
an attorney, bent upon bringing an action
against the author of the defamatory
article in last Sunday's Examiner.
"It was nothing but lies," he said,
"from beginning to end. That man Mil- :
lard did it out of spite. The Occidental
boys are not the only ones from whom lie i
tried to collect money for water from
the spring I leose. When I allowed him
to connect his pipe with the spring I told
him that the fact that he paid $4 a year
rental would nor. warrant his interference
with any campers to whom I might rent
grounds. I made him understand distinct
ly that the campers were first as far as
that spring was concerned. How did he 1
live up to that agreement? Last season I
he allowed the owners of two houses, |
! Shultz and Kllllp, to tap his pipe and us* |
; my water, for which he charged them $21
a month apiece. He made the same deal j
with John Gormley. This year he tried It j

FLEET OF TUGS
SEARCHING FOR
THE LOG RAFT
Insurance Men and
Owners Anxious.
The derelict lop raft is giving shipping
men and the local underwriters a great
deal of uneasiness. The big mass of piles
is somewhere right in the track of ves
sels bound up and down the coast, and is
therefore a serious menace to navigation.
There Is over fix million feet of lumber
in the structure and there is about $75,000
insurance on it. Hence the uneasiness of
the underwriters.
If the raft is to be picked up it should
be heard from within the next forty-eight
hours. The Fearless may have picked it
up, and if she has will bring it into port.
Early yesterday morning- the Czarina
went out again and later in the day she
was followed by the tugs Rescue and Sea
Que*n. The latter vensel will tow the
schooner Norma to Fort Bragg and as
soon as that job is accomplished she will
Join the Rescue, Fearless and (.."zarina in
the search for the- derelict. The steamer
President of the Alaska Packers' fleet
will probably go out to-day, ho that five
steamers will be searching for the raft
by this afternoon. A number of vessels
reached port yesterday, but none of them
saw a trace of the derelict. It is still
somewhere between Point Arena and
Point Reyes but no one has been able
positively to say that he saw it since the
ship Oriental bo nearly ran it down. Yes
terday the steamers Robert Adamson,
Noyo, State of California and Glenogle all
arrived from northern ports, but not one
of them caught sight of the raft.
Captain George Harvey went out in
command of the tug Sea Queen, as Cap
tain "Ike" Smith is confined to his home
With a serious Illness.
The cruiser Marblehead was towed to
Mare Island by the navy yard tug Una
dilla yesterday. She will go on the dry
dock for an overhauling and will come
out of retirement some time next month.
The Oceanic Steamship Company's mail
steamer Australia arrived from Honolulu
yesterday. She brought up a full cargo
and about fifty cabin passengers and over
100 in the steerage. The passage was a
very pleasant one and the run was made
in six days and twelve hours. The cabin
passengers were:
Mr and Mrs. Atwater, five children and
servant. Mr. and Mrs. J. B. Atherton, A.
Atherton. A. F. Afong, Mr. and Mrs. H.
G. Boswell and daughter, Mrs. E. Benner,
Miss Benner, Miss M. Burns, Thomas
Buckley, B. R. Banning, Mayor G. G.
Cornich, Mrs. S. L. Dexter. Mrs. Dv Rbi,
Mr. and Mrs. A. Enos and son, C. Eccles,
H. P. Franklin, Mrs. C. Graham and two
children, L. T. Grant. W. B. Godfrey Jr.,
Mrs. W. L. Howard, A. Hazzard, J. M.
Hewson, Mrs. W. G. Irwin and maid,
Miss Helene Irwin, Mrs. Keating, Mr.
Klrkland, W. Lucas. Dr. and Mrs. Mour
itz and three children. J. E. Murphy, Mr.
Marion, D. A. Ray. W. T. Rawline. Mr.
and Mrs. George W. Smith and child, C.
G. Smith, T. M. Starkey, H. G. Spencer,
L. F. Stone, Mr. Steinhart, Mrs. C. D.
AV right, Mrs. F. M. AVakefield, C. F.
Wall, Mr. Withers.
Ignatz Steinhart returns after a visit to
Honolulu in connection with the establish
ment of the First American Bank of Ha
waii. Mrs. AY. G. Irwin comes to San
Francisco in connection with the erection
of her palatial home on Pacific Heights
and also to look after the education of her
daughter, Miss Helene Irwin. A. F.
Afong, brother of Mrs. Captain Whiting
of the United States navy. Is going to
Yale to complete his studies.
Harry Nichols, a 13-year-old lad who
returned from Honolulu on the Australia,
has an ardent desire to get to Manila.
He left his home in Missouri over six
months ago and made his way to San
Francisco. When the transport Pennsyl
vania sailed Harry was aboard as a stow
away. AVhen discovered he was placed
under arrest, but before he could be hand-
I ed over to the United States Consul at
| Honolulu he made his escape. After the
Pennsylvania got away young Nichols
got work on the United States steamer
Iroquois and finally a passage back here
on the Australia was arranged for him.
AVhen Harry walked down the gangway
of the steamer yesterday morning he had
$1 7n in his pocket, but he says that is
enough to keep him until he can stow
away en another transport.
Three of the salmon fleet are making
long passages. The bark Coryphene is
now out 30 days, the Merom 24 days and
the ship W. H. Macy 31 days from Bristol
Bay. Head winds and calms have prob
ably delayed them, and to-day or to-mor
row they may show up off the heads In a
bunch.
The steamer Nippon Maru sailed for the
Orient yesterday with a valuable cargo
and a fair passenger list. There was the
usual crowd down to see her off.
The British tramp steamer Glenogle,
that has lately "been carrying merchandise
from china for the Pacific Mail and
Northern Pacific, arrived from Tacoma in
ballast yesterday. She is under charter
to the Government, and will be turned
into a transport as quickly as possible.
Joe Fountain, a newsboy, was treated at
the Harbor Hospital yesterday by Drs.
Robinson and Murphy for a serious cut in
the leg. He was sitting on a doorstep on
Stevenson street, between Second and
Third, when a band of young hoodlums
came along. Their leader was John Wel
don, and he had an open pocket-knife in
his hand. "Watch me make this guy
jump," he remarked to his companion,
and with that he stuck the blade of the
knife into Fountain's thigh. With that
the crowd ran away and Fountain was
taken to the Harbor Hospital. The police
are looking for AVeldon.
The San FYancisco owned ship Eclipse
is back in Sydney harbor, much the worse
for an encounter with a hurricane. She
Bailed from Newcastle. N. S. W., for Hon
olulu with a cargo of coal, and soon after
she got out a terrific storm came up. The
cargo shifted, the seams opened and at
one time it looked as though the vessel
would go down. AVhen the storm abated
she was leaking pretty badly, but the
crew finally managed to get her into Syd
ney.
There are only three disengaged vessels
in port, and they would not be idle a mo
ment if their owners would accept the
ruling charter rates. They are the Brit
ish ships British Isles, Cambrian Hills
and Claverdon. All of them are holding
out for 2s 6d a ton more than the offering
rate. At this time last year there were
over twenty disengaged vessels in port.
HOW ALFALFA LAND IS
ASSESSED IN MERCED
They assess valuable land very low
down in Merced County, and the State j
Board of Equalization does not know of I
it. Samuel O. Crittenden. a respectable
farmer whose place is near the thriving
hamlet of Ingomar, let the cat out of the
bag yesterday morning when giving tes
timony before United States Commission
er Iloarork in the matter of the San Joa
quin and Kings River Canal and Irriga
tion Company against the County of
Stanislaus, itn Board of Supervisors and
others. The Supervisors fixed the rates
j to be charged by the corporation at a flg-
I ure which the irrigation company declares
i virtually amounts to a confiscation of its
i property.
Mr. Crittenden corroborated the testi
mony of the other witnesses as to the al
falfa industry in that part of the country.
He told how it cost about $7 or $S per acre
to prepare the ground and put it in al
falfa, and 25 cents an acre to keep tho;
checks in ordor and put the water upon
the land, exclusive of the cost of the
water, and that for the first five years of
' cultivation the yield was from seven to
! eight tons of alfalfa hay per acre, the
hay bringing in the nearest market from
$4 to $4 50 per ton.
"What is the average value of land with
alfalfa on it in your neighborhood?" was
asked.
"About $100 per acre. I'd hate to have
to take $100 per acre for mine."
"What is your land assessed for?" was
the next question.
The witness blushed slightly as he re
plied "sl2 per acre."
The smile that went round the court
i'room was an indication that the land of
the other witnesses had been treated
equally as leniently by the Assessor as
Mr. Crittenden's.
The taking of further testimony in the
case will be continued during the next
two days.
